23.     Despiteprogressin social indicators,Burkina continuesto rank towards the
bottom of the Human Development Index and the country is unlikely to reach
several MDGsby 2015. Despite improvements, Burkina Faso maintains a comparatively
low ranking of 173 out o f 179 countries inthe UNDP HumanDevelopment Index. And
while Burkina Faso is on track to reachthe MDGtargets for access to potable water and
reduced HIV prevalence, significant challenges exist with respect to attaining the rest of
the MDGs, in particular those related to literacy, gender, infant mortality, sanitation and
maternal health. Ineducation, the literacy rate reached only 28.7 percent in2007 and the
quality of education has remained low. Inhealth, mortality figures remain alarming with
very highinfant andunder-five mortality, respectively at 104per 1,000 and 191per 1,000
in 2007. Of particular concern is the deterioration in malnutrition rates over the last
decade. Burkina also compares less favorably than other countries for assisted births
despite efforts to improve healthprovision. Burkina's unbridleddemographic growth rate
of 3.Ipercent represents a further obstacleto humandevelopment. The global crisis risks
having a negative impact on the availability of public resources for basic services and
progress towards the MDGs.



  111. BURKINAFASO'SPOVERTYREDUCTIONSTRATEGY (PRSP-11)

24.     BurkinaFaso's second povertyreductionstrategyembodiesthe mediumtermvision
of the country. Developed in 2003, Burkina's second PRSP aims to maintain real GDP growth
above 4 percent per annum, halve poverty to 35 percent and increase life expectancy to at least
60 years by 2015. The four pillars of the strategy consist of: (i)raising growth and equity in a
stable macro-economic environment to reduce poverty; (ii)     increasing access to and quality of


                                              - 10-

basic social services; (iii)
                           improving employment andincome opportunities for the poor; and (iv)
improving governance with a particular emphasis on public sector management and budget
management reforms. The Government has maintained steady progress in implementing the
PRSP, as demonstrated in the three Annual Progress Reports. Particular achievements have
included investment climate reform, public finance and budget management, anti-corruption
measures and improved access to education and water. However, progress in improving public
sector management, judicial reforms, diversification and the decentralization process have been
slower.

25.     Moving forward, the Priority Action Plan for 2009-2011, which guides the
implementation of the PRSP, targets investment climate reforms and private sector
development, improved public expenditure, employment and revenue generation in rural areas,
accelerated decentralization and enhanced public financial management. Agricultural
productivity i s a central priority. The Government has recently released a concept note on the
proposed Strategy for Accelerated Growth and Sustainable Development which i s intended to
replace the PRSP in2010 -11.

26.     Burkina Faso's longer-termvision focuses upon human security (economic, health,
food, environmental,individual and political) and poverty reductionand is encapsulated in
the country's Sustainable Human Development Policy o f 1995 and the national prospective
`Burkina 2025' of 2001. The long term strategies envisage regional and global integration;
sustainable economic growth; increased public expenditures to accelerate human capital
development; and strengthened decentralization and modernization processes in government.
More recently, a Government-sponsored Etude du Sche`ma National d 'Ame`nagement du
Territoire has mapped future scenarios for Burkina Faso's development. The study identifies
three fundamental long term strategic objectives: (i)    deceleration o f demographic growth and
empowerment / advancement o f women; (ii)     human capital development; and (iii)   increased and
intensified agricultural production and self-sufficiency. The Government's Green Revolution
establishes the road-map for the development o f competitive, market-integrated agricultural
production by 2015.

 IV. BANK GROUP'S SUPPORT TO THE GOVERNMENT'SPROGRAM

A.      LINKTOTHE COUNTRYASSISTANCESTRATEGY

27.     The latestCAS sets out the BankGroup's supportto the Government'sPRS for the
years FY06-FY09. The CAS is fully aligned with the PRSP-I1 and contributes to specific
country outcomes: (i)expanded and diversified export earnings; (ii)an improved investment
climate; (iii)efficient economic infrastructure; (iv) improved access to quality basic services; (v)
increased access to potable water and sanitation services; (vi) enhanced labor market
functioning; and (vii) improved public resource management in a decentralized context.            It
foresees the use o f three types o f instruments: development policy operations (PRSCs),
investment credits, and analytical and advisory activities (AAA). The proposed PRSG-9 i s an
integral part o f the CAS as it i s designed to provide financing for the budget and support the
implementation o f policy reforms in key areas targeted by the CAS, namely to: (i)     improve the
investment climate and promote exports; (ii)    protect access to basic services; and (iii)
                                                                                          promote
efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use o f public resources. Assistance provided


                                               - 11-

by the PRSG-9 is particularly relevantnow as the country is trying to cope with the impact ofthe
global economic downturn and protect critical developmental objectives o f PRSP-11.

B.      RESULTSACHIEVEDUNDERPREVIOUSPRSCSAND LESSONS                     LEARNED

28.     The government made significant progress during2007-09 (the implementationperiod of
this third series of PRSCs, notably PRSC-7 and 8) in its efforts to improve: (i) investment
                                                                                     the
climate and promote exports; (ii) to quality services through deepened decentralization;
                                     access
and (iii) the use o f public resources through enhanced public financial management. Progress
made and key results achieved are detailed below.

Competitiveness,ExportDiversificationandInvestmentClimate
29.     At the height of the 2007 cotton crisis (which involved a 44 percent drop in cotton
production, 14 percent slump in producer prices, reductions in farmer revenues and increased
indebtedness o f cotton farmer cooperatives), and in response to Government requests, IDA'S
response was escalated. PRSC-7 and PRSC-8 placed particular emphasis on supporting the
cotton sector, particularly for the recapitalization o f SOFITEX, the implementation of a new
producer price mechanism, technical and financial support to the re-privatization process, and
support measures to consolidate the cotton sector beyond the crisis. The Agriculture
Diversification and Market Development Project (ADMDP) also supported increased
productivity and farming system diversification initiatives. Given the external climate and the
significant vulnerability o f the cotton sector, sustained efforts to support ongoing reforms and to
stabilize performance will be crucial to the viability o f this sector.

30.     The PRSP series identified economic diversification as paramount to alleviating the
over-reliance on the cotton sector and to expanding export earnings, but progress has been slow.
The Diagnostic Trade Integration Study (DTIS) suggested several measures to improve prospects
for export diversification beyond cotton to products such as cattle and meat, sesame, gold, zinc,
mangoes and craft products. Support under the PRSC-7 was directed towards the adoption and
implementation o f an export promotion strategy.           PRSC-8 supported the emerging export
potential in gold mining by providing assistance to Burkina Faso on its involvement in the
Extractive Industries Transparency Initiative (EITI). Miningproduction grew from 7.4 to 9 tons
between 2006 and 2007 and i s projected to grow by a further 10 percent in 2008/09. The PRSC
series has also been directed towards assisting the Government to encourage increased private
sector investments. However, despite government efforts exports have stagnated at 9 percent o f
GDP over the last decade, with the share o f cotton exports rising from 48 percent to over 60
percent. To achieve diversification, the government faces the significant challenge o f addressing
a multitude o f interrelated constraints including poor infrastructure, the high cost o f public
services and utilities and inadequate policies and institutional frameworks for private investment
and export promotion. Giventhese, the diversification process is likely to take time.

31.     The PRSC series has supported government progress in improving the investment
climate. Through the PRSC series, the Government has improved contract enforcement,
established two Business Registration Centers (CEFORE), reformed construction permit
processes and reduced business registration formalities. The effects o f these extensive reforms
are reflected in the consistent improvements in Burkina Faso's ranking in the Doing Business
indicators. Burkina Faso was ranked as one o f the top ten Doing Business reformers in2009 for


                                                 - 12-

its business regulatory reforms. The PRSC series also supported increased labor market
flexibility through several decrees in 2007 which regulated overtime, dismissals and working
hours. As a consequence, the Doing Business Labor Market Rigidity Index rating for Burkina
has fallen from a last-place standing o f 90 to 61 in 2008 (although this i s still well above the
regional average o f 42.6 and the CAS 2009 target o f 50). PRSC-7 also supported reforms to
enhance security o f land tenure and provide infrastructure at lower cost. Inenergy, an important
country outcome promoted by PRSC-7 was to improve the legal and regulatory framework to
encourage private sector and donor participation in the sector. The adoption o f the Electricity
Law inNovember 2007 i s a key result on the institutional front. However, structural reforms to
the electricity utility, SONABEL, as well as to parts o f SONABHY have lagged and require
acceleration. A key future challenge will also be to ensure the rapid implementation o frural land
reforms.

Decentralization and Delivery of Basic Services
32.      Decentralization. Decentralization has featured as an important element inthe last two
PRSC series. Under PRSC-6 the framework for the transfer o f resources and competencies to
communes was finalized. Subsequent credits were directed towards the implementation o f the
framework. The IDA-funded Administrative Capacity Building Project has strengthened central
institutional capacity in the context of the decentralization process in four cross-cutting central
ministries. The IDA-funded Decentralized Urban Capacity Building Project strengthened the
capacity o f the main urban municipalities to take on the responsibilities transferred to them
through the decentralization process with support to urban management capacity, financial
resource mobilization and financial management of municipalities. Support was also given to
institutions implementing decentralized urban development in establishing sustainable transfer
mechanisms and strengthening national level capacity. The series assisted in the Government's
creation o f a fund for investment transfers (Funds Permanent Pour Le De'veloppement des
Cdectivite's Territuriales) in April 2007. However, progress on the decentralization front has
been slow and the mechanisms for ensuring adequate transfers from central to municipal levels
are still intheir infancy. Weak capacity has also constrained the decentralizationprocess.

33.      Health. PRSCs have contributed to a constructive health policy dialogue with the
government over the CAS period.         This included support towards the establishment of a
medium-term expenditure framework (MTEF) for the sector, a system o f obstetrical emergency
subsidies and policies to reduce the cost o f assisted births. Health services have become more
accessible. Positive trends in the health sector include improvements in vaccination coverage,
reduced mortality rates from meningitis from 21 to 8.5 percent between 2005 and 2006,
reduction in HIV/AIDS mother to child transmission rates and a significant rise inthe treatment
o f people living with HIV/AIDS (beyond Government targets). Worrisome indicators despite
improving trends include insufficient coverage with regard to geographical access, staff and
equipment and the persistent highrates o f unassisted deliveries and acute malnutrition.

34.     Education. The Bank's PRSC series contributed towards the finalization o f the
consolidated MTEF for the sector, as government worked to integrate primary and secondary
education and increase efficiency in educational spending. Regular increases in education
expenditures as a share o f the GDP from 2.1 to 2.4 percent between 2004 and 2007 have allowed
the Government to expand teacher training and upgrade schools with running water and



                                               - 13 -

sanitation facilities. Significant progress was achieved by the Government inimproving accessto
education, although issues relating to quality o f education and disparities incoverage continue to
exist at all levels. The benchmark indicator for increasing national gross enrollment rate in
primary education to 70 percent in 2008 was reached, however important disparities still exist
between urban and rural zones.         The quality of education services represents an ongoing
stumbling block and is reflected in the low literacy rates (below 30 percent), teacher
absenteeism, poor studentto teacher ratios and the low level o f teaching hours (640 hourdyear in
2007 on average compared to the national requirement o f 900 hours). The Bank is engaging
comprehensively in education policy dialogue through the Education for All Fast Track
Initiative, which will assist the Education Ministry in implementing the decentralization agenda
inthe sector.

35.      Water delivery and sanitation. The PRSC series has supported legislative amendments
to enhance private sector participation inwater delivery in secondary urban and semi-rural areas.
Support o f village water management and social water connection programs through the
development o f public-private partnerships (PPP) between communities and the local private
sector has yielded results. Burkina Faso i s on track to meet the MDGs related to access to
potable water. Access to potable water has risen to 77 percent inurban areas and 54 percent in
rural areas. The PRSCs advanced a new water management framework that strengthened the
institutional development and management capacity o f the national water utility, ONEA,
resulting in increased bill collection, improved productivity o f the commercial staff and
alignment o f annual financial reports with international standards. Notwithstanding progress in
this sector, important disparities remain in access to safe drinking water in rural communities.
The PRSCs also assisted with the development o f sanitation strategic plans for four secondary
cities. However these have not been implemented and sanitation plans for other cities are still
outstanding. The Government has identified lack o f human resources as the main constraint to
improving the implementation o f sanitation programs.

PublicFinancialManagement,GovernanceandEnvironment

36.     Public Financial Management. The Government made considerable progress in the
context o f public resource management with the introduction o f a broader integrated action plan
to strengthen public finances in 2007 (SRFP), the introduction o f computerized expenditure
management systems in the regions, the preparation o f timely reports on budget execution and
adoption o f program budgets, MTEFs inkey sector ministries, and the introduction o f a software
and I.T. network to track public procurement. The PRSC series has facilitated Burkina's marked
progress on four main pillars o f reforms in public financial management: (i)   budget preparation;
(ii)budget executionandtransparency; (iii) procurement; and(iv)internalandexternal
                                                public
oversight, including the fight against corruption.

37.     On budget preparation, the PRSC series has paved the way for a progressive move
towards greater focus on performance. While PRSC-7 focused on the alignment o f the budget
allocation with the MTEF and PRSP priorities, PRSC-8 resulted in the introduction o f program
budgets intwo pilot ministries (infrastructure and environment). In2008, the authorities decided
to scale-up these pilots and move towards full-scale program budgeting. However, several
factors pleaded for caution in the implementation of this ambitious objective and justified a
pause in the focus o f the PRSC series on budget preparation. Burkina has already gone through


                                                - 14-

an unsuccessful attempt at introducing program budgeting a decade ago. This created some
degree of skepticism in ministries, which could only be overcome through strong political
leadership and in-depthtechnical preparation. A recent IMF mission (May 2009) has outlined
conditions needed for successfully implementing program budgeting in Burkina Faso. AfDB
and other donors have expressed willingness to provide technical assistance in this area. The
authorities also wanted to wait for the revision of WAEMU PFM directives to be completed
before moving further, inorder to ensure consistency of the reforms envisagedwith this regional
legal framework. Once the road map towards program budgeting is clarified, the PRSC series
couldresume its support to this pillar.

38.       On budget execution and transparency, PRSC-7 supported the implementation of
functional budget classification with a marker for poverty-spending. More recent PRSCs are
now progressing from supporting the production of relevant information to its dissemination.

39.       On public procurement, the focus has progressively shifted from reforming the legal
and regulatory framework (Pillar I)           to focusing on strengthening the procurement capacity and
the transparency o f the procurement system. The Government advanced in the implementation
of procurement reforms with the adoption of an action plan based the finding of the 2005
Country Procurement Assessment Review (CPAR), the setting up of a new legal and regulatory
framework with the adoption of a new procurement Code and the issuance of procurement
regulations and Standards Bidding Documents. PRSC-7 supportedthe strengthening of indicator
4 of Pillar I1with the creation of the Regulatory agency for public procurement" with the decree
no2007/ 243 PRESPMMFB, and the revision of its attribution, composition and functioning.
PRSC-8 focused on the establishment of procurement units in two regions: the Hauts-Bassins
(Bobo Dioulasso) and the Center East Region (Tenkodogo); and in two line ministries: the
Ministry of Basic Education and Literacy and the Ministry of Health. Sole sourcing for public
contracts was considerably limited as a procurement procedure by the Government, dropping
from 13 percent of all public contracts in 2007 to 3 percent in 2008. In addition, procurement
contracts have been regularly audited by the Inspection Ge'ne'rale des Finances (IGF). Though
important steps have been taken to improve procurement and financial management, the
experienceinBasic Education reveals continuedproblems inimplementation of country systems.
A Basic Education audit is being conducted to identify logjams and Burkina Faso has been
selected as a pilot country for initiatives to strengthen country procurement and financial
managementsystems. The government has indicated commitment to this process.

40.       The fourth axis of public financial management reforms in this PRSC series has
supported an overhaul of the internal and external oversight systems. Building on
achievements of the previous PRSC series, which supported the creation of an independent
Supreme Audit Institution, PRSC-7 focused on capacity-building within the Court of Accounts.
PRSC-8 reinforced the independenceof the Court by protecting its budget from in-year freezes.
It also created a system for delegatedaudit of small communes' accounts by regional treasurers,
on behalf of the Court. These measures securedessential conditions for the Court's independence
and effectiveness. The Court of Accounts benefits from a strong technical assistance program
funded by multiple complementary sources. The European Commission is financing a medium-
term capacity-building that focuses on institutional strengthening. IDA supports the Court

 ~~    ~~




Io Autorite' de Re'gulation des Marche'spublics ( A M P )


                                                         - 1 5 -

through several instruments: the Public Sector Reform Project supports the acceleration of the
Court's program to judge accounts correspondingto years preceding its establishment; the Public
Control Institutions IDF supports the hiring of private sector auditors to cross-train the Court's
auditors and help them undertake financial audits; the Bank has also organized with GAC
funding an international workshop aimed at strengtheningthe relationships betweenthe Finance
Commission of Parliament and the Court. The Bank i s also financing technical assistance from
the Moroccan Court of Accounts, inthe form of peer review services to assist the BurkinaCourt
implement some of its reforms. Furthermore, the creation of the Budgetary and Financial
Discipline Chamber i s supported by the French cooperation through non-lending technical
assistance. Finally, given that 68 percent of audit reports in FY08 were considered not
acceptable, an audit firm assessed the Chartered Accounting Body in May 2009 and identified
specific actions to strengthen-up the capacity of both public sector control institutions and
private audit firms, whichwill be supportedthrough another IDF grant.

41.     Following the adoption in May 2006 of a national anti-corruption policy, the
Government committed to step up efforts to fight corruption with the establishment of an
independent audit institution, the High Authority for State Oversight (Autorite' Supe'rieure de
ContrGZe de Z'Etat, ASCE). All these efforts have combined to help improve significantly the
country's ranking on the 2008 Transparency International's Corruption Perception Index to the
80th position (from 105 in 2007, out of 179 countries). Burkina Faso remains among the better-
rankedAfrican countries.

42.     Environment. The Ministry of Environment prepared in 2007 a National Capacity SeZf
Assessment with support from UNDP and GEF, which provides detailed information on
environmental issues the country is facing, as well as its needs in meeting the objectives of
addressing climate change biodiversity, desertification, and sustainable development more
generally. It also identifies all the agencies working inthese areas in the country and estimates
their capacity needs. To linkthis effortwithinrealistic budgetingparameters, PRSC-8 supported
the preparation of aprogram budget for the Ministry of Environment.

K e y Lessons
43.     A set of lessons relevant to the proposed operation was drawn from the ICR of the
previous PRSC series."      Inparticular, the ICR noted that PRSCswere an effective instrument
to support cross-cutting reforms and improve sectoral dialogue. The current operation noted that
successful economic diversification depends on overcoming a multitude of obstacles
(infrastructure, private sector development, transport, export policies) and requires the adoption
of a tightly integrated approach. The proposed PRSG-9 continues to support this key objective
needed for sustainable poverty reduction, while enhancing the integration of such support with
complementary Bank and other donors' interventions. In parallel, the M&E framework of this
operation, in line with recommendations from the ICR, has increased its focus on qualitative
improvements, particularly on agricultural productivity, while streamlining the coverage of
outcome indicators. Finally, technical assistance is more systematically provided to enhance the
capacity of the administration to executeprograms inthe PRSC areas.




  Report No: ICR0000621, February20,2008.


                                               - 16-

44.    Decentralization has been slow as the mechanisms for ensuring adequate transfers from
central to municipal levels and weak capacity have constrained its implementation process.
While donors have supported the policy o f decentralization, financial assistance has been
concentrated at the central and community levels. The proposed operation includes enhanced
support for accelerating financial transfers to strengthen the decentralization process, particularly
inthe education sector, where it is complementingthe Fast-Track Initiative.

45.    Institutional capacity continues to be a stumbling block to aid effectiveness and
development. Significant weaknesses in the implementation o f national financial management
and procurement systems represent key constraints to harmonized sectoral approaches and
pooled funding mechanisms. At the same time, the multiplication o fproject implementation units
(PIU) financed by donors (currently over 500 in the country) severely taxes Government's
capacity. While PRSG-9 has renewed its focus on public financial management reform and the
improvement o f country systems, IDA will also rationalize its portfolio to reduce the number o f
PIUS.

46.    General budget support needs to identify a clear institutional anchor for reforms.
Consistent progress was made in areas under the direct control o f the central ministries, notably
the Ministry o f Economy and Finance, whereas progress was uneven where reform
implementation depended upon the involvement o f particular line ministries and on the capacity
of the Ministry o f Economy and Finance to coordinate sectoral reforms. Consequently, PRSG-9
has opted for a more narrow sectoral coverage than its predecessors, particularly on service
delivery.

c.     COORDINATION WITH OTHERDEVELOPMENT               AGENCIESAND THE IMF

47.    Provision of donor support in a coordinated and cohesive way, in line with the
Government's overall development strategy, is evolving in a positive way. Twenty seven
donors currently support the implementation o f the Government's PRS through various modes o f
assistance: general budget support (GBS), sector programs, investments projects, capacity
buildingprojects and technical assistance. Investment projects account for two thirds o f external
aid, while budget support accounts for approximately one third o f total donor support. GBS
accounted for US$268 million in 2008, with IDA providing about 34 percent o f this amount.
The three multilateral institutions (IDA, EU and AfDB) are providing altogether about three
quarters o f GBS to Burkina Faso.

48.    PRSG-9 has been designed in the context of the joint Cadre Giniral d'orgarzisation
des Appuis Budgitaires (CGAB), established in 2005 to deepen harmonization o f nine donors
(the European Union, the Netherlands, the African Development Bank, Sweden, France,
Denmark, Switzerland, Germany and IDA) inthe context o f budget support through alignment o f
policy advice, ajoint performance matrix and annual progress reports (Box 2). The specific areas
for PRSG-9 support have beenidentified     inclose coordination with other development partners,
in aiming for synergy with their respective interventions, and building on IDA'Scomparative
advantage in these areas. Donors will continue to strengthen their partnership by promoting,
through the General Budget Support Framework process, best practices, better communication,
information sharing and coordination, Government ownership, and improving predictability o f
financial support.



                                                - 17-

                                 Box 2: Donor CoordinationEIarmoniation


   Burkina Faso was a pilot country for the initiative on the Test of New Forms of Conditionality, implemented
   from 1997 to 2000, under the stewardship of the European Union. In contrast with old practices where
   conditionality was driven by donors, the new initiative tried to strengthen recipient country ownership and
   responsibility for policies (design, implementation and monitoring-evaluation). A major lesson from this
    initiative was that budget support provided through this new form contributed to improve ownership,
    accountability and the use of country systems and result-basedmanagement principles.

    Owing to low administrative capacity and divergent and multiple practices on the side of the donors, transaction
    costs related to the management of budget support were significant for the Government. The first attempt to
   harmonize donor activities dates back to 2002 with the signature of an agreement (SBC-CSLP) between the
    European Union, Denmark, France, Germany, the Netherlands, Sweden and Switzerland with Government
    approval. IDA and the AfDB became active members of the initiative in a later phase, while the IMF has an
    observer status and can participate on a voluntary basis. In February 2004, the Government proposed a revised
    and more inclusive version, bringing all budget support partners into the Cadre Ge'ne'rald'organisation des
   Appuis Budge'taires (CGAB, an organizational framework for coordinated budget support). Focused on (i)
    ownership; (ii)alignment; (iii)  predictability; and (iv) a result-based approach, the CGAB is built around ajoint
    performancematrix (Annexes 10-15) which is the principal tool for coordination and evaluation.

    The CGAB monitors and evaluates permanently but has three mandatory sessions each year, with a formal
    performance session held at the time of the annual review of the PRSP progress report. These sessions are
    chaired by the Minister of Economy and Finance, supported by a secretariat made up of a representativeof the
    administration and two representatives of the donors. Joint missions are planned to reduce administrative
   transaction costs. The new institutional PRSP M&E system is advancing its integration into the country
   broad institutional M&E system.

    On the basis of three-year experience, the preliminary conclusion is that the CGAB requires further work to
    rationalize the joint policy matrix to focus it on strategic milestones of the government's policy. Future work
   will, among other, focus on further improving the PRSP monitoring and evaluation system and aligning the
    CGAB and PRS processes.Inparticular, the operation o fthe sectoral and thematic Commissions (CST), which is
    the anchor of the PRSP M&Esystem, needs to be strengthenedand reliable data made available to better inform
   the performance indicators. As regards donor performance, the principal challenge is still related to the
    predictability ofbudgetarysupport for which the PEFA report, carried out inJanuary 2007, offers a C+ mark.

    Results from the 2008 Survey on Monitoring the Paris Declaration show that the Bank has made substantial
    progress toward the Paris Declaration targets. In particular, it is on track to meeting its targets for use of public
    financial management (PFM) systems and has made progress on increasing lending through Program-Based
    Approaches (PBAs) as well as conducting joint analytic work. The Bank's experience in donor harmonization
    during the current PRSC series has raisedthree particular issues: (1) harmonization relies upon effective national
    leadership; (2) the implementation of country systems requires significant strengthening to enable greater
    integration of Bank support; (3) programmatic approaches depend upon the development of common
   understandings amongst donors with respect to fiduciary and monitoring and evaluation expectations. The World
    Bank is also leading initiatives to develop ajoint assistance strategy upon the completion ofthe next PRSP.




49.      The IMF and IDA staffs have coordinated closely on the identification of the
respective institutions' areas of focus, with the IMF taking the lead in the policy dialogue on
macroeconomic policies and monitoring macroeconomic performance by way o f quantitative
performance criteria and indicators, while the Bank takes the lead in long-term growth, social
and poverty analysis, sectoral issues (notably cotton), and public expenditure analysis. In a
numberof areas wherethe mandates ofthe two institutions overlap, especially inpublic financial
management, there is close coordination and collaboration to ensure that consistent advice is



                                                            - 18-

provided to the authorities. The IMF Board i s expected to discuss the fourth Government's
semiannual PRGFperformance review on June 22,2009.

50.     IDA has now aligned its PRSG-9 missions with the CGAB calendar and process. It
has moved to a joint monitoring and evaluation matrix, evaluating whether disbursement criteria
are met. IDA has improved its field representation and involvement inthe sectoral and technical
commissions reviewing the PRSP-I1 implementation. It has also improved coordination with
donors on a continuous basis and moved away from the mission model inthe preparation o f this
operation. IDA also collaborates with several other partners inthe context o f sectoral programs
which are integrated in the national budget mechanism. Last, the coordination o f technical
assistance inthe field o f capacity strengthening i s one o f Government's major focus.

51.     To further decrease the transaction costs of harmonization, the government is
proposing to consolidate the CGAB and the PRSP review processes. The existence o f two
separated and parallel monitoring and evaluation (M&E) processes, one for the PRSP and the
other one for budget support tended to increase the transaction costs related to donor
harmonization. In order to streamline the M&E system, the government took the leadership in
February 2009 and proposed a draft decree that creates a new PRSP M&E set up aiming at
integrating the two processes. This set up envisions three levels o f M&E: the first level will build
on the existing sector review frameworks; the second level i s the Steering Committee; and the
third is the Council of Ministers. On their side, donors started organizing themselves around a
TROIKA (three representatives) which will be the unique interlocutor o f the government.
However, for this year's annual review, since the sector review frameworks do not exist for all
the sectors, the government had to draw on the existing sectoral and thematic commissions
(CST) with the mandate to also cover budget support issues.

52.     While most of the gains from harmonization, namely a reduction in transaction
costs and predictability of aid, have now been achieved in Burkina Faso, PRSCs have often
demonstrated the frequent need for exceptional measures in response to economic shocks,
that require more flexibility than formally allowed within the CGAB framework.                  This
year, PRSG-9 i s supporting the Government's response to mitigate the impact o f the global
economic downturn on the Burkinabi: economy, particularly in the cotton sector. The reforms
PRSG-9 supports could not have been anticipated earlier or would not have gained sufficient
government and stakeholders' ownership without the sense of urgency that the crisis brought
upon. While it i s critical that PRSCs continue to bringpredictability indisbursements based on a
joint policy matrix agreed at year N-1,they are at times called to help the Government address
emerging priorities and support new policy measures through flexible consultations o f donors
and stakeholders.

D.      COMPLEMENTARITYWITH OTHER BANKACTIVITIES

53.     Several IDA-financed operations are direct complements to the PRSCs 7-10 series
and provide financial and technical assistance inthe areas supported by the proposedPRSG-9.

54.     IDA has provided technical and financial support in relation to the first component
of the PRSC series on competitiveness and export diversification through the
Competitiveness and Enterprise Development Project (US$3 1 million, FY03), in particular with



                                               - 19-

the provision o f business development services to strengthen capacity at the firm level and to
promote adoption and adherence to international norms and standards, especially for exporting
firms. In the energy sector, IDA has a two-pronged approach to support the provision o f low-
cost energy.    First, IDA and other donors have financed urgently-needed power generation
capacity in Burkina, as well as investing in regional electricity interconnections with C6te
d'Ivoire and Ghana. The goal has beento halve the cost o f electricity. Second, IDA's Power
Sector Development Project (US$64 million, FY05) i s providing technical and financial support
to increase rural access to modern energy sources, through expansion o f the electrification grid
and investment inalternative technologies, Also, IDA's Energy Access Project (US$39 million,
FY08) i s offering support to the Government's efforts to increase energy services expansion to
rural, peri-urban, and urban areas. Inthe transport sector, IDA, with the Transport Sector Project
(US$92 million, FY03) is part o f a consortium o f donors assisting the Government in
rehabilitating and maintaining essential transport infrastructure.      The support also involves
strengthening existing institutions and adopting sound policy and regulatory measures. At the
regional level, the West Africa Transport and Transit Facilitation Project (US$6 million, FY08)
is aiming to improve access to Ghanaian ports by facilitating the efficient movement o f traffic
along the Tema-Ouagadougou-Bamako transport corridor.

55.      In agriculture,the IDA-financed Agricultural Diversification and Market Development
Project (US$66 million, FY06) i s providing assistance complementary to the PRSC series by
supporting increased productivity in the critical cotton sector, the development o f promising
agricultural sectors and supply chains (fruits and vegetables, cattle/beef, poultry) and i s financing
expanded irrigation and measures to diversify farm outputs. IDA i s promoting private-sector led
irrigation to allow dry-season production o f high-value export crops such as tomatoes and
onions. IDA also supports the manufacture and maintenance o f small scale irrigation equipment,
as well as institutional and financial support. The project aims to double the surface o f irrigated
land inthe project zone and increase beneficiary (including women's groups) household income
by an estimated 30 percent. IDA supported agricultural intensification and marketingprogram is
now scaling up earlier investments in export infrastructure, such as cold storage rooms at airport.
The Agricultural Productivity and Food Security Project, planned for the first quarter in FY10,
will support agricultural production by improving access to productivity-enhancing technologies.
The project will also focus on broader food security issues by improving post-harvest storage
facilities and by targeting food access constraints. The Irrigated Agriculture Development
Project, also planned for FY10, will develop irrigation-based agricultural supply chains by
increasing irrigated areas, enhancing crop and animal productivity and value-added in selected
catchments' areas, and improving the operation and maintenance as well as the financial
sustainability o f irrigated systems. At the regional level, the Africa Emergency Locust Project
(US$8 million, FY05) which closed inFY09, aimed to reduce the vulnerability o f Burkina Faso,
as well as other neighboring countries, by supporting the development of a regional early
warning and response systemfor locust infestation.

56.      Complementary support for the decentralizationprogramis being provided through
the second Community Based Rural Development (CBRD) Project (US$74 million, FY07) that
helps build rural communities' governing and management capacities and the Decentralized
Urban Capacity Building project (US$lO million, FY07) which supports decentralization in six
urbanareas. IDA is also providing complementary support, jointly with other donors, for sector
wide approaches in basic education, health, HIV/AIDS and water supply. In health, a pooled


                                               - 20 -

funding mechanism channels resources directly to health districts and hospitals on the basis of
indicators that can be tracked over time. The HIV/AIDS Disaster Response Project (US$22
million, FY02) and the Health Sector Support and AIDS Project (US$63 million, FY06 and
additional financing inFY08) have contributed a total o f US$85 million equivalent to the sector.
In the education sector, IDA has contributed with the Basic Education Sector Project (US$51
million, FY02 and additional financing inFY08) and the Post PrimaryEducation Project (US$23
million, FY06) to expand children's access to basic education. At the primary education level,
IDA projects target girls and the poor inthe provinces with the least coverage. As administrator
for the Education For All Fast Track Initiative Catalytic Fund in FY09, the World Bank will
remain engaged inpolicy dialogue with the Government on basic education objectives, including
the decentralizationof education service delivery. IDA'Scontribution through the Ouaga Water
Supply Project (US$70 million, FYO1) financed the secondary and tertiary water network inthe
capital which resulted in a tripling o f the number o f people having direct access to piped water.
The project closed on December 2007.           IDA has also invested in drainage and sanitation
infrastructure in Ouagadougou and Bobo-Dioulasso, with positive measurable effects on disease
prevention and economic activity in surrounding areas. A New Urban water and Sanitation
project programmed for FY09 will target urban water supply for the cities o f Ouagadougou,
Bobo-Dioulasso, Koudougou and Dedougou, urban sanitation in Ouagadougou and Bobo-
Dioulasso and institutional development and capacity strengthening. The project will form part
o f a coordinated donor response and will support Burkina Faso in attaining the urban water
MDGby 2015 (87 percent access).

57.     The Administration Capacity Building project (US7 million, FY05) supports an
agenda that is complemented by the PRSC series by providing assistance for capacity
building inpublic administration for policy formulation and implementation, streamlining public
administration, modernizing personnel management, and enhancing the poverty focus o f public
expenditure.    The Statistical Capacity Building project (US$lO million, FY04) addresses
shortcomings inthe M&E system by strengthening data collection and reporting. Finally, at the
request o f the Burkinabk authorities, IDA i s assessingBurkina Faso's country systems.

E.      ANALYTICALUNDERPINNINGS

58.     The PRSG-9 is underpinned by considerable analytical and fiduciary work
undertaken by the Bank, the Government and other partners. To inform policies aimed at
accelerating growth, the proposed PRSG-9 draws from Bank, Government and other partners'
economic and sector work. The upcoming C E M on growth, competitiveness and diversification
prepared by the Bank highlights the critical importance o f reforms in the agriculture sector,
notably cotton, and in removing growth bottlenecks to move up the value chain on several other
sectors, including in the livestock/meat sub-sector. The C E M also highlights the key role o f
measures to improve infrastructure, the business environment and services to small and medium
enterprises that informed the choice o f policy measures supported by the proposed operation.
The C E M findings build on a report on the sources o f growth and the vulnerability o f Burkina
Faso's Economy to exogenous shocks (Government - MEDEV, December 2005) and are
complementedby a number o f sectoral reports (Organization and Performance o f Cotton Sectors
in Africa: Learning from Experience, the World Bank, 2009), by preliminary findings of the
financial and organizational audits o f SOFITEX for 2004-2007 and by the 2007 Diagnostic
Trade Integration Study (DTIS), which already identified meat as one o f the promising sub-


                                                -21 -

sectors susceptible to improve prospects for export diversification beyond cotton. The labor
market study conducted in2006 identifiedpriority reform for spurringemployment that provided
relevant insights into the choice o f policies supported by the PRSC series. The 2006 Investment
Climate Assessment and annual Doing Business Reports highlighted concrete micro-level
constraints to Burkina Faso's growth, including ICT, that have been instrumental in the PRSC
series' design. These findings are further corroborated by a recent report on foreign direct
investment policy prepared by UNCTAD (2009).

59.     The Making Decentralization Work study in 2007 identified key challenges in this
area. The report concluded that, at the central level, the complex set o f decentralization and de-
concentration structures requires further rationalization and that the mechanisms for
intergovernmental transfers need to be clarified to ensure appropriate, regular and transparent
transfer o f resources to the communes. The report also clarified that at the commune level,
limited basic capacity inplanning and budget management constitutes a major obstacle and that
local revenue collection and adequate staffing also need to be addressed.             In addition to the
decentralization ESW, the World Bank commissioned a political economy analysis in 2008,
which concluded that to improve accountability and transparency the Government needs to
revisit the freedom of information framework and develop appropriate avenues for engaging with
civil society and the media. The 2009 Education Sector Review has assisted the Government in
readjustingthe national program towards the development o fthe education sector. On rural water
supply, analysis undertaken in the 2008 Regional Water and Sanitation Public Expenditure
Reviewhighlightsthe importance o f maintenance to sustain the gains made inexpanding access
to drinking water. Both studies helped deepen understanding o f two key sectors for the
decentralization process and informed the measures proposed in the PRSC series to improve
access to quality basic services. The ongoing PER takes stock o f recent trends inpublic spending
and proposes ways to leverage the decentralization process in order to improve public service
delivery ineducation?health and rural development. Some o f its early findings on the importance
o f transparency and accountability at the local level have informed the PRSG-9 program.
Lessons from the policy response to the food crisis highlightedthe need for analytical work on
safety nets in Burkina Faso (a study on social safety nets programs i s ongoing) to inform policy
actions to strengthen them and which could be supported by the next PRSC series.

60.     Finally, considerable analytical effort has been conducted in assessing the
effectiveness of public spending and public finance management in Burkina Faso, including
the 2005 CPAR and the 2007 Public Expenditure and Financial Accountability (PEFA)
exercise.12 The design o f the current PRSC series also benefited from other studies, including a
recent report on the diagnosis o f the Supreme Audit Institution (Cour des Comptes) and its
Action Plan (European Commission, 2006), an IMF Report on Improving Budgetary
Management (2007), an EU audit o f reform implementation to improve public finance
management, a 2009 peer review report o f the Burkinabe Supreme Audit Institution by its
Moroccan counterpart and the ongoing PER. These studies helped identify the strengths and
shortcomings inBurkina Faso's fiduciary system and served as inputs for the authorities' update
o fthe budget management reform program (Programmede Re`forme de la GestionBudge`taire).

                              V. THE PROPOSEDPRSG-9

A.       RATIONALEAND OBJECTIVES

61.      The proposedoperation is the third in a series of four annual PRSCs (PRSCs 7 to
10) to support the Government's PRS implementation. The proposed operation aims to assist the
authorities' efforts to cope with the impact o f the global economic downturn and maintain the
momentum o f reforms to achieve critical developmental objectives.         While PRSG-9 would
translate primarily into funding for the PRSP-I1implementation, particular attention is given to
addressing the cotton sector crisis exacerbated by the global economic downturn and sustaining
the implementation o f selected PRSP-I1 reforms to support growth, protect basic service
provision and enhance the impact o f public resource use.         The development objectives o f
PRSG-9 are to:

     (9       improve the investmentclimate and promote exports with a particular focus on
              actions to save the coming cotton campaign and carry out longer term reforms to
              improve the sector's overall competitiveness;

     (ii) protect access to quality basic services by further operationalizing
              decentralization in basic education and water and sanitation and by developing
              municipal investments; and

     (iii)    promote efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of public
              resources through increased information on budget execution and transfers to local
              governments and through stronger external control institutions (the Court o f
              Accounts and the HighAuthority for State Oversight).

B.      RESULTFRAMEWORK

62.     Government development objectives and outcome indicators for all budget support
operations have been jointly selected by members of the General Budget Support
Framework (CGAB) in Burkina Faso. Indicators for the period 2008-2010 are presented in
Annex 14 and their definition and measurement is spelled out indetail inAnnex 15. Inlight o f
the harmonization process, these indicators aim at outcomes that are broader than those that can
be strictly expected from the policy measures supported by this operation. The Government
development objectives to which this operation will specifically contribute and the outcome
indicators that will be usedto assess progress are bolded inthe indicators matrix.

63.     In the process o f compiling the CGAB matrix for 2008-2010 some performance and
monitoring indicators proposed for PRSCs 7-9 in the PRSC-7 document have been dropped,
although they remain highly relevant. To monitor outcomes o f the PRSC series relative to three
objectives: (i) growth and reduction inthe incidence of poverty; (ii)
                real                                                     increased diversification
of exports; and (iii) medium-term financial viability o f cotton ginning companies, the PRSC
                      the
series will follow a limitednumber o f additional indicators not retainedby the CGAB as reported
inAnnex 3. It should be noted the outcome for these indicators have been adjusted for 2009 to
reflect the exogenous impact of the global crisis which has a dampening impact on the economy
despite continuation o f the Government's policy reform agenda.         The majority o f donors,

Annex 3.


C.       POLICYAREAS

64.      This section discusses the Government's policy reforms supported by this third
PRSC series and presentsthe rationalefor the selection of prior actions for PRSG-9and of
program triggers for the next operation (PRSG-10). While this series was originally
envisaged as including three PRSCs (PRSC-7 to 9), it i s proposed that PRSG-IO become part o f
the current series. The Government expects to complete preparation o f the next PRSP next year.
The new PRSP would then be endorsed following presidential elections also scheduled for 2010.
Itis therefore preferable to wait for the newPRSP to define the objectives andcontent ofthe new
PRSC series and continue to provide support to the authorities' reform program with PRSG-10
under the current series.

Improvingthe InvestmentClimateandPromotingExports

65.      The cotton sector is and will remain a vital element of Burkina's economy in the
short to mediumterm and efforts to stabilize it and improve its productivityand viability
need to be accelerated. Burkina Faso is sub-Saharan Africa's biggest cotton producer with
unprocessed cotton accounting for above half (in good years) to above a third (in bad years) o f
exports. The sector provides 700,000 jobs, employs 17 percent o f the population and constitutes
the main source o f cash revenue for many farmers. The cotton sector is at once Burkina's success
story and her Achilles heel. Reforms in the cotton sector have produced a model for integrated
supply chain development and have resulted insignificant growth inthe sector. From 1995/96to
2005/06 cotton production increased by 17 percent a year, against 2.1 percent in the world as a
whole. However, high commodity price volatility, low productivity and the sector's vulnerability
to climatic shocks have resulted inlow and unstable income generation.

66.       The global downturn,the declinein domestic cottonprices and difficultiesto place
forward contracts on markets have significantly affected the profitability of ginning
companies and cotton farmers' livelihood,and have put pressure on government finances. As
a result o f the crisis, cotton companies are threatened with bankruptcies, farmers and village
cooperatives are engorged in debts, and in some areas up to 30 percent o f the producers have
renounced planting cotton in the coming campaign. The internal debt overhang i s estimated at
CFAF4.4 billion (or more than US$8 million). Approximately two-third o f this amount was
accumulated during the 2007-08 season when cotton production suffered from an unexpected
drought toward the end o fthe productioncycle. Inline with existing governance rules governing
repayment o f the debts due by producers' cooperatives (known in Burkina as Groupements des
Producteurs de coton, GPC), it is the surplus farmers who ultimately pay for the debts of their
fellow GPC members who are indeficit. There i s thus a significant risk that farmers abandoning
cotton cultivation are those more competitive, thereby causing a potentially disproportionate
reduction in overall production volumes that might further hurt processing capacity utilization
ratios, thereby further undermining the competitiveness o f cotton firms. Inview o f this, there i s
a need to resolve internal GPC debt while at the same time avoiding moral hazard. Given the


                                              - 24 -

currently high fertilizer prices, efforts to promote explicit fertilizer subsidies are also needed to
ensure a successful 2009/2010 cotton campaign.

67.         The Government is putting together a sound emergency package to respond to the
crisis, with a focus on immediate measures to save the coming cropping campaign
(2009/2010). The government's objective is to encourage seed cotton production at its
2008/2009 level (452,000 T) inthe coming campaign, without distorting long-term incentives set
by the price-setting mechanism.                With a farmgate price fixed at CFAF160/kg this coming
campaign (Le. 3 percent less than a year earlier), while a large share o f the farmers remain
indebted to their cooperatives, the Government i s committed to increase its subsidies for
fertilizers, subject to availability o f funds, so that input costs to farmers remain unchanged from
last year. This explicit subsidy would amount to CFAF7 billion (US$14 million equivalent),
which i s CFAF4 billion more than last year. The Government is committed to remove the
internal debt overhang at the producers' level without encouraging moral hazard. Clearing all
internal debts of producer solidarity groups, as the Government envisages, would amount to
CFAF4.4 billion (or nearly US$9 million). To deal with incentives issues looking forward, the
Government will request a revision o f the insurance system among producers to both provide
higher ex-ante collaterals and cap individual liability to the farmers' group so that surplus
farmers keep part o f their benefit even in bad production years.                        Finally, to guarantee
SOFITEX'S business continuity inview o f deteriorating solvability, the Government will launch
a comprehensive financial restructuring of SOFITEX and increase by CFAF16 billion (around
US$32 million) its credit line to the cotton company, which constitutes a contingent liability for
the state. Direct budgetary expenditures directed to the coming cotton campaign would add-up
to US$23 million.

68.        The Government is also working on medium-term measures to restore the cotton
sector's viability by improving its overall competitiveness. To restore the profitability o f the
cotton sector, the Government i s accelerating the use o f GM cotton, a new technology for which
recent tests have indicated that on-farm productivity could be increased by 30 percent, and that
four pesticide treatments per campaign could be saved, while at the same time overall production
volumes would increase above levels required to arrive at unit processing costs that are
internationally competitive. Measures to improve managerial efficiency o f the company based
on recommendations provided by a recently concluded financial and operational audit will be
proposed within a three-year action planto address the crisis inthe sector.13 The development o f
insurance schemes, particularly against extreme weather events, and the enhanced capacity o f the
cotton companies to engage in forward transactions will likely constitute options proposed to
minimize the effect o f volatility at the micro and macro levels. The Government is aware that the
smoothing fund i s rapidly depleting its resources and that recapitalization o f the fund might be
warranted at the end o f the coming cotton campaign in 2010. Recent analytical work had

69.     Economic diversification outside of the cotton sector is paramount to reduce
vulnerability, enhance export performanceand ensure sustainable growth in the medium
to longer term. Burkina Faso's low income level and small internal market makes it dependent
upon exports for growth. Yet the country's 10 percent export over GDP ratio between 1998 and
2004 was the lowest of all WAEMU countries and fell well below the average regional rate of 31
percent.   In this respect, harnessing the potential of the agriculture sector, improving the
investment climate and promoting small and medium enterprises are critical elements of the
Government's agenda.

70.     In the medium term, agriculture (includinglivestock)is the most obvious source of
growth and poverty reduction in Burkina Faso. The agriculture sector employs over 80
percent o f the population and contributes to 40 percent o f national GDP. Between 2001 and
2008, growth in agriculture exceeded the national average despite year to year fluctuations.
However, agricultural growth over the past decade has predominantly relied upon area
expansion, a practice that cannot be sustained due to fast-approaching spatial saturation, rapid
demographic growth and land degradation. Productivity has remained stagnant, due to very low
level use of productivity-enhancing technologies, including irrigation. Subsistence farming is
widespread, with limited access of producers to both internal and external markets and minimal
involvement of agribusinesses in the sector. Environmental degradation and climate change
have had particularly ravaging effects on the performance of the agriculture sector. To harness
the potential of the agriculture sector, the government intends to intensify agricultural
productivityby improving development, distribution and access to yield enhancingtechnologies,
enhancingnatural resourceand risk management, strengthening landtenure and commercializing
products by developing tightly integrated and efficient agricultural supply chains gearedtowards
regional export markets. Intensifying agricultural production will enable Burkina to develop its
domestic market thereby achieving food security and reducing the country's dependencyon food
imports in a time of global recession. It will also create the necessary conditions for a shift from
subsistencefarming to diversification into high income-enhancing agriculture.

71.    Livestockhas beenidentifiedas a promisingsubsector for export diversificationand
growth. Growing urbanization and rising per capita income have led to stronger domestic
demand for meat, coming on top of export demand. Supply has not coped well with this rising
demand. Closing the domestic supply gap will require increased productivity per hectare in
extensive systems or per animal in intensive systems, through improved nutrition, breeding, and
animal disease control. Expanding exports would require addressing food safety issues at the
sub-regional, regional, and, in the longer-term, international level. Better compliance with the
sanitary and phytosanitary requirements of the World Trade Organization, which are based on
Livestock International Organization (OIE) (live animals) and Codex Alimentarius (animal
products) guidelines and standards, will be crucial to this sector's growth. The Government's
specific strategy is described inthe Work Plan and Investment Program for the Livestock Sector
(PAPISE) - a national framework for livestock action planand investment programs preparedby


l4
 Burkina Faso: Analysis o f the Cotton Price Smoothing Fund (Fond du Lissage) by Gauresh Shailesh
Rajadhyaksha, John Baffes and StephenMink,November 2007.


                                                    - 26 -

 the Ministry on Animal Resources in2000 - and inthe PRSP-11. The Government's objective i s
 to complete the development o f livestock resources (cattle, pork and poultry) by genetically
 designing highly productive, resilient and high value-added races; using manure to enhance soil
 fertilization, defense and restoration; and promoting mechanization. The Government expects
 that these measures will have a significant impact on poverty reduction as smallholders are
 producing about 95 percent of total meat production and livestock-related activities provide
 incomes (partially or totally) for 86 percent o f the population. Itwould also address the needs o f
the poorest areas inthe country, the NorthEast, where livestock production i s concentrated.

 72.     Private sector participationinthe economy is weakened by the low capacityof small
 and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), for which improving the investment climate
 remains a top priority. Development o f the private sector, including implementation o f an
investment promotion strategy and access to finance for Small and MediumEnterprises (SMEs)
 are crucial to growth and diversification. The government has achieved significant progress in
improving Burkina Faso's investment climate. Its progressive reform agenda and sound
privatization and liberalization platform has resulted in the state divestiture o f 15 private
companies, improved land tenurehegistration policies and reforms to reduce labor market
rigidity and streamline business registration. Comparatively, however, Burkina Faso still ranks
poorly in the Doing Business indicators (148 out o f 181 countries in 2009) and the upcoming
C E M identifies a decline inthe investment growth rate from 2.6 percent between 1994 and 1999
to 1.6 percent between 2000 and 2006, with further reductions possible inthe wake o f the global
financial crisis. Despite positive efforts to support private sector development, SMEs continue to
face significant constraints, including the absence o f business incubators for emerging
enterprises and capacity, cost and access issues associated with existing enterprises. Inparticular,
the 2009 Doing Business report ranks Burkina 113thout of 181 countries for the business start up
indicator, listing an average requirement o f 5 procedures, 16 days and a cost equivalent to 62
percent o f the income per capita. The Government intends to further simplify administrative
barriers for enterprise creation by extending its regional network o f single administrative window
for small and medium enterprises.

73.      The government is now eager to improve access to ICT services and develop
innovative applications that will facilitate improved public service and growth of local
private industries. Burkina Faso has embarked on far reaching initiatives to deregulate and
liberalize its telecommunications sector. These efforts led to increased investments. Currently,
the telecommunications sector contributes close to 4 percent o f GDP (2.3 percent in 2001) and
generates over 35,000 jobs. Telecommunications penetration rates have increased from 5.5
percent in 2005 to 14.5 percent in2007. Over 85 percent o f the country i s currently covered by a
mobile signal. But the developments in the telecommunication sector mask inadequate and
uneven access to basic and advanced ICT services. In particular, the lack of a robust national
fiber optic network and access to competitively priced international bandwidth impedes the
development o f high-speed Internet services (broadband services). Burkina's Internet users
represented 0.5 percent o f the population in 2007. Hence, the internet penetration rate is lower
than the Sub-Saharan African average o f 18.3 percent, while prices continue to be high. The
hourly connection to Internet (CFAF1,180) is higher than in Mali (CFAFSOO) or Ghana
(CFAFSOO) but still lower than in Benin (CFAF1,320) and Senegal (CFAF1,770). As in most
African countries, the high cost o f Internet services reflects a mix o f cost and profit factors. This
i s mainly the result o f over-dependence on satellite technologies and absence o f competitive


                                                - 27 -

access to the submarine SAT3 cable via C6te d'Ivoire and Senegal. Burkina has embarked on an
effort to connect its national network by optical fiber to CBte d'Ivoire, Mali and Togo networks.
It is also expectedto benefit from a regional initiativeto improve regional broadbandaccess and
interconnection. While the ICT regulatory framework is still under development, Burkina Faso i s
committed to modernizing its legal framework.

Focus of the PRSG-9andplannedfollow-on operation(PRSG-10)

74.      The first component of the PRSG-9 supports policy actionsjudged to be critical to
improve the investment climate and promote exports. In the cotton sector, the PRSG-9
supports the following efforts to improve the competitiveness of the cotton sector: (i)                     the
consolidation o f the price-setting mechanism for cotton through the contracting of an
independent agency taking full responsibility for implementing the formula and calculating the
farmgate price each year; (ii) contracting of a credit rating agency to provide rating advice for
                                the
SOFITEX; (iii)   the production of certified accounts for SOFITEX's activity in 2008; and (iv) the
implementation of SOFITEX's restructuring plan to reduce the company's operating costs, as
adopted in2008.

75.      The enforcementof the pricemechanismhasbeenconsolidatedinresponseto minor
slippages endured in the determination of the 2008/09 farmgate price. The authorities
applied the price mechanism in good faith using the ICAC projection for year N+1twice while
they should have used World Bank projections for year N+2 (Table 3), leading to a floor price of
CFAF165/kg of seed cotton. With the decline in world cotton prices and subsequent losses of
cotton companies, no supplement was paid to farmers at the end of the season. The AICB
recognized that it had been poorly advised by its cotton consultant, traditionally in charge of
calculating the farmgate price. To avoid further slippage in the future, the Bank of Africa was
contracted by the Association o f the Smoothing Fund, the newly created authority applying the
price-setting mechanism, to calculate the farmgate price each year, starting with the 2009/2010
season. The floor price proposed for the 2009/2010 cropping season (FCFA160kg of seed
cotton) reflects the strict application of the formula using the appropriate price projections as
defined by the Smoothing Fund's governance rules. Ina context of low commodity prices, the
strict respect of the rule on the determination of the farmgate price will limit potential losses for
the Smoothing Fund and cotton companies, reducing the build-up of the government's
contingent liability resulting from the global economic downturn.

76.     The Ministry of Economy and Financecontracted Global Credit RatingCompany,
registered in Johannesburg, to provide rating advice on SOFITEX. The rating advice will
provide further input for the government's three-year action planto stabilize the cotton sector.

77.     While SOFITEX was able to submit audited accounts for 2008, the company has
revealed negative equity since 2006 at least (- CFAF19 billion in 2006; - CFAFl1 billion in
2007; - CFAFIO billion in 2008). This situation poses serious risks on the viability of the
company.lSIn response, the Government envisages a financial restructuring of the company,
which would avoid triggering bankruptcy as per OHADA rules. The budgetary impact of such a

78.        InDecember 2007 SOFITEX adopted a plan to reduce its operating costs (Table 4).
FCFA5.8 billion was achieved in cost reduction between 2007 and 2008. This represents 2.7
percent of the company's 2007 operating costs. While the draft audits o f SOFITEX suggest
additional niches for cost reduction, results achieved so far testify to the company's
responsiveness in time of financial and economic stress. A continued focus on improving
SOFITEX's competitiveness, a company that controls 85 percent of the cotton produced in
BurkinaFaso, would have a lasting impact on the efficiency of the whole sector.

79.        Ina context that is less favorable to growth and where commodity prices are expectedto
remain under pressure from the global economic downturn, PRSG-10 would support the
adoption by the Council of Ministers and its progressive implementation of a three-year
Action Plan (2009-2011) for restructuring and supporting the cotton sector. Progress in
implementation will be assessed with respect to measures aimed at increasing productivity,
improving input access, restructuring financially SOFITEX and strengthening the company's
performance, four reform areas that are full components of the PRSG-10 trigger.                                   The
Government will provide implementation reports on a quarterly basis that will be reviewed inthe
new Consultation Framework for all stakeholders. Inparticular, meetings will be called at the
onset of the cotton campaign to review how the farmgate price was set in accordance with the
Smoothing Fund formula, and toward the end of the campaign to assess outcomes and
profitability and discuss needed reforms. This regular consultation would support consensus
building among stakeholders and donors and enhanced ownership on cotton sector reforms. It is
expected that the three-year action plan would address underlying structural problems of lack of
competitiveness of the sector, measures to deal with the existing debt overhang for farmers,
management requirements for cotton companies, embeddedin a wider agreement involving the

The ICT legislation will facilitate the development o f e-commerce and the launch o f e-
Government. By establishing clear rules on authenticity and responsibility o f digital signatures,

facilitate government's archiving tasks and digitalization o f administrative processes, simplifying
people's access to the administration, limitingcommuting requirements, improving transparency,
traceability and efficiency. As a result o f this new legislation, increased demand for internet
access, including from the administration, is expected to boost efforts to diversify broadband
access and interconnection on a more competitive basis, resulting over time into lower hourly
connection costs, more in line with the regional average. The opening o f two new regional o+ne-
stop-shop centers, or CEFORE (Centre de Formalitks des Enterprises), will result in immediate

procedures and reduction o f informal payments incurred by several administration counterparts.
Entrepreneurs or investors in the above two regions can now interact and accomplish the
procedures with a sole administration."              Revenue collections for fiscal and social security
administration are also improved by having more formal enterprises with fiscal identification
number and social security registrations. The package offered by CEFORE not only reduces
procedures for business creation but also contains a set o f primary capacity buildingadvices and
workshops for new entrepreneurs to strengthen their skills. They will be located within the local
antennas o f the national business association (Maison de 1'Entreprise du Burkina Faso, MEBF),
which already offers counseling services and matching grant funds for consultancy services and

81.      PRSG-10 would support two other policy measures to further improve the
investment climate and promote exports: (i)              launch o f a public tender for the management o f
the refrigerated slaughterhouse in Ouagadougou by an experienced technical partner; and (ii)
taking priority actions to sustain the activity o f the Maison de I'Entreprise du Burkina Faso
(MEBF,.        Support for the livestock sector has been provided through the Agricultural
Productivity and Market Development project (PAFASP). Overall, there has been good progress
inaddressing the supply chain development. DemandofBurkinameat comes from Cote d'Ivoire,
Ghana, Egypt, and DRC. On the production side, PAFASP worked with producers to improve
the fattening methods. About 5,000 animals were under short cycle-3 months fattening, so that
at least 15,000 animals be produced per year with the improved techniques. While meat
production i s improving, exports o f chilled or frozen meat i s being hampered by inadequate
preservation facilities and competition from other producing countries. A cold chain i s available
for air road and train transportation. But the infrastructure o f the slaughterhouse and needed
professionalization o f its management were identified as growth bottlenecks. A study conducted
by CNUCED proposed a set o f key investments to upgrade the slaughterhouse to international
norms. In parallel, the Government has decided to seek an experienced strategic partner to
improve the management o f the refrigerated slaughterhouse in Ouagadougou. It i s expected that
