82.     The MEBF was created to offer counseling services and matching grant funds for
consultancy services and training for business.       The MEBF provides information, advocacy
services and targeted business development services to over 4,500 private sector personnel. In
support o f diversification, the MEBF recently opened an office dedicated to assisting exporting
enterprises and is working with the Agriculture Diversification and Market Development Project
(ADMDP) inthis regard. The Government intends to award to the MEBF public utility status,
issue the title deed o f its headquarters, and establish a fund for capacity building in the private
sector that will be managed by the MEBF(all elements that are a full component o fthe PRSG-10
trigger). The award o f public utility status will allow the MEBF to benefit from subventions,
some exemptions and to better mobilize resources among donors. Finally, the issue o f the title
deed will give the MEBF full property rights on the public land it currently occupies.

Protecting access to quality services through deepened decentralization

83.     In Burkina Faso, the decentralization agenda is central to protect the quality of
public service delivery. A legal framework and administrative decrees were introduced and
priority responsibilities covering most primary service delivery functions (primary education,
safe water supply, basic health services, etc.) were transferred to the newly elected local
governments.     Following the Decentralization Law o f 2004 (Code Ge'ne'ral des Collectivite's
Territoriales), elections for local governments (Collectivites Territoriales - CTs) were held in
2006.    However, the decentralization o f basic services has taken longer than expected to
materialize during the operational phase o f the decentralization process.       While transfer o f
competencies to communes has been granted, resource transfers are only just commencing.
Improving incentives to motivate education and health professionals to move to rural areas
through the decentralization process represents a particularly challenging issue.         Also, the
framework for de-concentration and decentralization lacks clarity and capacity i s lacking at the
local level. In particular, CTs continue to lack the means and resources to properly plan and
implement transferred mandates and activities.

84.     The government adopted in 2007 a new Strategic Framework for Decentralization
Implementation (CSMOD in French) to accelerate the implementation pace. The revised
decentralization framework covers the period until2015 with a rolling three-year implementation
planregularly reviewed. Inparticular, the CSMOD focuses on: (i) transfer o f competencies
                                                                      the
and resources (human, budgetary, fiscal and patrimonial); (ii)the strengthening o f local
capacities (of the basic service units, the local governments and the deconcentrated branches o f
the state administration); and (iii)the coordination o f the decentralization process itself (by
coordinating donor harmonization, providing sustainable financial support, improving land,
environmental and natural resource management, and improving local development).                The
government's main achievement in 2008 has been to gather sectoral data, prepare a draft action
plan and organize several consultation workshops to sponsor ownership o f the decentralization
process.




                                               - 32 -

 85.     Advancing the decentralization agenda will require increased resources to local
 governments to perform in relation with their new responsibilities, while a sustainable
 transfer formula is being developed.                 Given the paucity of resources raised by local
 governments, local development in Burkina Faso will depend essentially on the volume of
 intergovernmentaltransfers, at the exception o f Ouagado~gou'~and Bobo-Dioulasso, which each
 raises annual revenue above US$400 million on average. Intergovernmental transfers currently
 include generic grants (dotations ge'ne'rales), fiscal transfers and the Standing Fund for the
 Development o f Local Authorities (Fonds permanent de de'veloppement des collectivite's
 territoriales or FPDCT).           Grants are split into operation grants (dotation ge'ne'rale au
fonctionnement or DGF) and investment grants (dotation ge'ne'rale d 1'e'quipement or DGE).
DGFis o f general unconditionaluse for the local governments' operating budget. It is calculated
 on a lump sum for each municipality and a complement commensurate with the population.
DGE is o f general use for local government's investmentbudget. It is based on the population
 size. The FPDCT, initially targeted at the 6 largest urban centers, also provides funding for
 investments and its access should be extended to all local authorities over time. Total
intergovernmental transfers for 2008 are detailed in Table 5. They accounted for CFAF13.6
billion intotal or 1.7 percent of total government expenditurein2008.

86.      In the education sector, the current strategy for decentralization puts emphasis on
empowering local authorities to deliver education services. The current approach i s to
gradually transfer management o f education to communes which, in turn, will manage schools
with assistance from the inspectorates on pedagogical aspects. The program offers a venue to
strengthen local governments, starting with their oversight on school construction, management
o f human resources, especially teachers, and making funds available at the local levels. The
communes will receive technical support from the center and/or outsource this function, notably
for school construction to ensure standard designs as well as technically and environmentally
sound buildings.

87.      Substantial capacity building of the local authorities will be essential to obtain the
desired developmental impact on student learning and efficient functioning o f schools.
Communes do not always have the capacity to develop and implement education plans at the


l9Between 2001 and 2004, Ouagadougou's internal revenue accounted for over 50 percent of all revenue raisedby
urban municipalities.
2oGrants for constructiono f local government offices.


                                                      - 33 -

local level, but they are entitledto assistance from the deconcentrated organs o f the Ministry o f
Basic Education (MEBA) when dealing with schools. However, the process for the local and
central authorities to adapt to their new roles will take time. In addition, the employment o f
teachers by local authorities presents incentives challenges.

88.     While the capacity of local authorities is being developed, capacity at the school
community level must also be built to improve the effectiveness of schools. Community
oversight of teacher performance at the school levels should continue to be encouraged, through
in-service and onsite pedagogical support o f teachers and schools, which are considered to
account for significant increases in teacher performance and classroom interaction over time.
Experience in other countries shows that without local support school pedagogical oversight is
generally weak, both trained and untrained teachers use the same unsuitable teaching methods
and teaching materials are used only in a perfunctory manner.21 One o f the factors that allows
improved results with the School-Based Management (SBM) model i s that local actors have a
greater incentive for their schools to succeed than institutions that are one or two steps removed
from school operations. For example, weak school leadership or poor quality teachers can be
quickly identified so that corrective action can be taken.

89.     In following the S B M model, MEBA has piloted the direct transfer of resources to
the schools.22 The Fonds Ecole initiative-piloted            in five provinces in 2004-05-transferred
grantsto the schools for purchasing school supplies. This SBM initiative made funding available
for goods that are typically underprovided (or underfunded) by the state and are thus typically
paid for by the parents, and funds are allocated through commercial banks to the provincial level
(Directionprovinciale de l'e'ducation de base et de l'alphabe'tisation, DPEBA), and then they are
distributed to the schools by the Circonscription d'e'ducation de base (CEB). The experience
from this pilot proved that schools in Burkina can manage funds quite well on their own. The
success o f the experiment has led the Ministry to scale up implementation o f the initiative to 20
provinces in2007. It is important that going forward efforts to empower local authorities as well
as implement the SBM model mutually support one another within the broader context o f the
decentralization framework.

90.     Despite significant improvements in urban access to water, disparities in rural
coverage and sanitation require greater focus. The government has made noteworthy progress
inimproving accessto potable water withthe development ofthe nationalprogram for water and
sanitation, improved public utility efficiency, implemented an ambitious and successful urban
extension program and developed public-private partnerships between communities and the local
private sector. While access in urban areas has increased significantly, a future challenge will be
to address disparities in rural coverage and focus greater attention upon the development and
implementationo f sanitationprograms. These are focus areas o fthe national program.

91.      Citizen, civil society and media participation as a means of bridging the
accountability gap and strengtheningthe demand-side for public service delivery is yet to
be fully exploited. To fill-in this gap, the government is committed to encourage access to
information at the municipal and service unit level and request full transparency in budget
execution.

Focus of the PRSG-9 and plannedfollow-on operation(PRSG-10)

92.      PRSG-9 supports government's efforts to advancethe effective exercise of power by
municipalitieson a pilot basis in two sectors (basic educationandwater and sanitation) and
the development of economic and market infrastructure in secondary urban areas, by
allocating resources for investment financing. Basic education and water and sanitation were
sub-sectors chosen for their direct impact on poverty alleviation, and their high intensity inboth
infrastructure management and social processes, with highlearning potential for decentralization
actors and the state. The measures selected are testing the solidity o f the system, and the true
capacity o f the actors to play their role in the efficient management o f local affairs. PRSG-10
would continue to support the decentralization o f basic education services as well as promote
transparency inlocal governments' budgets.

93.      On basic education, PRSG-9 supports: (i) disbursement on a quarterly basis of the
                                                          the
financial transfers to 49 urban municipalities inscribed in the 2009 budget law for recurring
charges; and (ii) transfer to territorial collectivities and disbursement on a quarterly basis o f
                  the
investment fund resources for classroom construction in the 13 urban municipalities that are
regional capitals. PRSG-IO would support the extension from 13 to the 49 urban municipalities
o f the transfer to territorial collectivities o f investment fund resources for school infrastructure
and its disbursement on a quarterly basis. These measures should have a substantive impact on
budgetary flows, as they entail the transfer o f recurrent expenses for the management o f 8,182
primary schools, and investment resources for the building o f 3,500 schools per annum on
average.

94.      On water and sanitation, PRSG-9 supports the signature of operation protocols
("pprotocoles d'ophation") for the management o f the services transferred to the territorial
collectivities inat least 100 rural municipalities (inthe aggregate). The `3protocoles" specify the
duties ofthe various players andthe administrative levels concerned; they define inmuch greater
details than the "arrite` de dkvolution" under which structures the transferred services will be
managed and map functions and responsibilities for all decentralized services. Inapplication o f
these protocols, FCFA200 million were transferred in April 2009 to the 100 rural communes for
the rehabilitation o f water pumps (or roughly US$4,000 per rural commune on average). The
General Directorate o f Water Resources (DGRE), through its deconcentrated services, will
provide technical assistance to the communes for contracting rehabilitation works.

95.     Finally, PRSG-9 supports the operationalization of the Standing Fund for the
Development of LocalAuthorities (FPDCT), which has started to select and finance economic
and social infrastructure projects in four municipalities beyond Ouagadougou and Bobo-
Dioulasso. To support economic activities and services in secondary urban municipalities, the
Government transferred CFAF5 billion to the FPDCT in 2008 and inscribed another CFAF5
billion in the 2009 budget, while disbursing CFAF1.5 billion during the first quarter.


                                                    - 35 -

Complementary technical assistance to the Fund was provided by the IDA-funded Urban
Development Support Project, known as Regional Development Poles (RDP), to improve the
FPDCT's absorption capacity at the municipal level. The initial purpose was to strengthen the
planningcapacities of the beneficiary towns, enable basic urbandevelopment activities financed
by the Fund, and improve the attractibility o f secondary urban areas, which is key to
decentralizationimplementation.

96.     While it is too early to fully assess performance of the Fund and its impact on the
ground, secondary urban centers have already started using this Fund to develop
infrastructure and living conditions. The FCFAS billionprovided to the FPDCT in 2008 were
allocated to six urbancommunes and for specific investment projects as detailed inTable 6. As
o f April 20, 2009, 24 percent o f the 2008 allocation had been disbursed to the communes.
Another FCFA5 billion were allocated to the FPDCT in the 2009 budget, o f which FCFA1.25
billion have already been transferred to the Fund.

97.     PRSG-10 would promote the publication on the Government website of the 2009
budget allocation and execution data of all 49 urban municipalities, broken down to the
level of sections and chapters. The 2007 PEFA report has highlighted significant shortcomings
both in terms o f the availability o f information on the resources received by service delivery
units (D score) and availability to the public o f more general budget information (C score). The
availability o f information on the resources received and spent by urban municipalities would
support increased financial accountability at the local level. This measure will enable civil
society and donors to help further disseminate budget and execution information in formats
accessible to a wider public (rural radio, local newspapers, and community-based interventions)
and ensure greater financial accountability, while focusing in particular on data pertaining to
primary service delivery units, such as schools and health centers. Beyond the dissemination

aspects, the local accountability component would be supported by on-going Bank supported
CDD and social accountability operations.

Promotingefficiency,transparencyand accountabilityinthe use ofpublicresources
through enhanced public financialmanagement

98.     Burkina Faso has been implementing an ambitious reform agenda since the
adoption in 2002 of a consolidatedaction plan to improve its budget managementsystem
(Plan d 'Actionpour le Renforcement de la Gestion Budge'taire, PRGB). Achievements to date
include: (i) stronger budget legislation which reflects most of the WAEMU directives on public
finance; (ii)improved alignment of budget allocations with PRSP priorities with the introduction
of a global MTEF; (iii)enhanced budget execution with the introduction of a computerized
expenditure management system that captures all domestically financed expenditures; (iv)
improved budget reporting and auditing with the introduction of an independent Court of
Accounts; and (v) the setting up of a new legal and regulatory framework with the adoption of a
new procurement Code and the issuance of procurement regulations and Standards Bidding
Documents.

99.     The 2007 PEFA report acknowledged these achievements but also highlighted
remainingweaknesses. Specifically, it identified scope for improvement inthe following areas:
(i)levelofmobilizationofdomesticrevenueandefficiency oftax administration; (ii)            public
availability of budget information; (iii)internal controls over salary and non salary expenditures,
including compliance with procurement regulations; (iv) efficiency and effectiveness of the
internal audit process; and (v) effectiveness of the external audit and parliamentary oversight
process.

100.    In February2007, the authorities approveda public financial managementstrategy
(Strate'gie de renforcement des finances publiques, SRFP) for 2007 to 2015. The three-year
rolling action plan that was prepared to implement this strategy (Plan d'Actions Sectoriel
Triennal,PAST) focuses on five priorities for 2009-2011: (i)    generatingmore domestic revenue;
(ii)implementing the public procurement reform; (iii)     supporting the decentralization process;
(iv) increasing the transparency of budget information; and (v) reinforcing audit systems and
fighting against corruption.

Focusof the PRSG-9andplannedfollow-onoperation(PRSG-10)

101.    This programmaticseries focuses on four mainpillars of reformsin public financial
management: (i)budget preparation; (ii)budget execution and transparency; (iii)public
procurement; and (iv) internal and external oversight, including the fight against corruption.
PRSG-9 focuses principally on budget execution and transparency and on external oversight.
The planned PRSG-IO would support three of these four priorities, namely increasing the
transparency of budget information, implementing the public procurement reform and
reinforcing audit systems andthe fight against corruption. This focus addresses some of the main
weaknesses identified by the 2007 PEFA and builds on prior successes of the PRSC series in
public financial managementreform.




                                               - 37 -

102.     The proposed PRSG-9 supports the publication on the government's website o f the
approved budget and information on budget execution (on a functional basis and for each
ministry) and on transfers to local governments.                 This paves the way for greater
accountability o f the executive, in particular through the involvement o f civil society
organizations inbudget execution oversight.

103.     PRSG-9 also supports the publication o f the annual reports of the Supreme Audit
Institution, the Court o f Accounts, for 2006 and 2007. The Prime Minister publicly
demonstrated his commitment to implementing the recommendations contained in these reports
on the occasion of their release, inJanuary 2009.

104.     Finally, PRSG-9 supports the publication of a baseline report on the extent o f
corruption in the country and the adoption of a statistical reporting system on pending and
closed corruption cases by the newly established High Authority for State Oversight
(ASCE). It further supports more attractive terms and conditions o f employment o f inspectorate
bodies' staff across ministries. With other Bank instruments (such as the IDF grant on oversight
institutions), the ASCE has begun to play its role as the apex of the internal oversight system, in
charge o f coordinating risk assessments o f all internal audit and inspectorate bodies, their annual
audit work plans and the follow-up o f the implementation o f their audit recommendations. It has
also started exercising its responsibilities inrelation to the identification, investigation, referral to
the criminal justice system and monitoring o f individual corruption cases. Technical audit and
inspection bodies were also strengthened by streamlining their institutional positioning in line
ministries and upgrading the compensation o f their staff. If the momentum o f this reform is
maintained, Burkina will be one of the first countries in francophone Sub-Saharan Africa to
adequately address the long-standing issues of institutional rivalries, inefficiencies and
ineffectiveness that have plagued the internal oversight system since independence and will
represent a good practice example.

105.     The planned PRSG-10 would support the deconcentration of the Directorate-
General for Procurement Contracts (DGMP) into at least three new ministries and three
new regions. This measure will be attested through: (i)the appointment by decree of
procurement experts in these new ministries and regions; and (ii)          the definition of thresholds
under which public contracts are reviewed by the deconcentrated procurement experts. The
recently adopted procurement legislation and regulation deals with the deconcentration o f the
procurement function. The issuance of a decree would help determine the actual delegation o f
procurement authority and functions performed to regional centers o f DGMP, at the exception o f
very large contracts. Also, in2008, the DGMP deconcentrated the procurement function with the
creation o f three regional branches (Hauts Bassins, Centre Est, Nord). PRSG-10 would support
the establishment in 2009 o f three new regional branches inBoucles de Mouhoun, Sahel and Est
and inthree ministries. At the request o f the government, the Bank will complement this support
withan evaluationo fthe country's overall procurementsystem and whether Burkina couldjoin a
pool o f candidate countries for the pilot program Use o f Country Systems in Bank-Supported
Operations.

106.     PRSG-10 would also support intensification inthe oversight o f the delivery o f goods,
works and services by the General Directorate for Financial Control (Direction Ge'ne'rale du
Contrde Financier, DGCF) in 2009 as evidenced by an increase in the proportion o f physical


                                                - 38 -

spot-check o f contracts subject to competitive bidding. The 2005 CPAR, 2007 PEFA report and
annual EC funded reviews of public procurement have shown that too many goods, works and
services are paid but are not compliant with specifications, not delivered in full, or even not
delivered at all. The certification process for goods, works and services delivery i s not watertight
- the DGCF, which is in charge of the oversight o f this process, does not devote enough staff
time and attention to this issue. PRSG-10 would support a significant increase from 2008 inthe
verification by DGCF o f actual deliveries for contracts above the competitive tender threshold.
This physical verification would occur prior to authorizing the payment o f related invoices. This
would increase the likelihood o f detecting collusion between suppliers and budget managers, as
evidenced by inexistentor inadequate deliveries o f goods, works or services.

 107.   Finally, PRSG-10 would support the set-up of the Budget and Financial
Accountability Chamber (Charnbre de discipline budgitaire et financi2re) of the Court of
Accounts and the starting of the review of cases as evidenced by a report from the Court of
Accounts.     The 2007 PEFA assessment has highlighted the lack o f follow-up to audit
recommendations and observations, as well as insufficient personal accountability, as major
weaknesses o f the Burkina's public financial management system. The francophone public
financial management system i s founded on the segregation o f duties between budget managers
and public accountants. Public accountants are held accountable for their management o f public
funds through thejudgment oftheir accounts by the Court of Accounts. Although its proceedings
have been marked by significant delays, the Court had, by the end o f 2008, produced 23 final
judgments on State and local authorities' accounts. The Court i s also receiving technical
assistance from an EC funded project as well as a Bank IDF grant to accelerate the pace o f
judgments and increase their quality. By contrast, the mechanism created by law to hold budget
managers accountable for their use o f public funds (the Budget and Financial Accountability
Chamber o f the Court o f Accounts) i s not yet operational. Even though the law allows the Court
to impose fines that could amount to twice the yearly salary o f public servants, these provisions
have never been applied. With its support to the Budget and Financial Accountability Chamber,
PRSG-10 would help create an effective mechanism to hold budget managers accountable for
their use o fpublic funds.

      joint performance matrix of the budget support group for 2009 (Annex 12). The detailed
      definition o f the measures (policy actions) proposed for 2009 by the CGAB framework i s clearly
      spelled out inAnnex 13, with a formulation that i s fully consistent with the proposedtriggers set
      out inTable 8 below.

                            Box 3. Good PracticePrincipleson Conditionality

Principle 1;Reinforce Ownership
This PRSG is embedded in the General Budget Support Framework (Cadre Ge`nkral d'organisation des Appuis
Budge`taires - CGAB), an organizational framework for coordinating and harmonizing budget support and its
process betweenthe Government of BurkinaFaso and donors engaged inprovidingbudget support. The CGAB in
its current form has been established in February 2005. It focuses on (i)    ownership of the reform process; (ii)
alignment with the country's PRSP; (iii) predictability of budget support; and (iv) a result-basedapproachwhich
is coordinated and agreedupon with the Governmentandthe donors providing budget support.

Principle 2: Agree upfront with the Government and otherfinancial partners on a coordinated accountability
framework
The group of donors organized in the CGAB support reforms as outlined in Burkina's PRSP and which are
updated and further specified in the annually revised Priority Action Plan (PAP). The PAP combines planned
policy objectives and actions with a mediumresource planning framework (MTEF) and a monitoring fiamework.
Followingthe PAP'Sobjectives and monitoringframework, the CGAB donors develop ajoint performancematrix
which i s the principal tool for coordination and evaluation of budget support. This procedure allows for
accountability along sharedobjectives andperformanceindicators.

Principle 3: Customizethe accountabilityframework and modalitiesof Bank support to country circumstances
Since 2005, IDA'Ssupport through PRSCs is part of the CGAB framework inresponse to Government demands
to align donor support to lower transaction cost for the Government and to harmonize the policy dialogue along
with the other donors around the PRSP and its priorities, as well as the accountability framework for budget
support. Identification, pre-appraisaland appraisal are completely aligned with the CGAB calendar which offers
three principal sessions with the Governmentto assess policy outcomes, jointly with an assessment o f the Annual
Progress Report of the Country's PRSP implementation (April), to identify policy measures and possible
conditions for budget support (October) and to conduct a midterm review (February). Only reforms truly owned
by the Government, however sensitive, have beenretainedinthe program.

Principle 4: Chooseonly actions criticalfor achieving results as conditionsfor disbursement
The policy matrix uses a selected set of nine prior actions for Board presentation. The chosen policy measures
were identifiedjointly with the Government and the other CGAB donors to ensure a critical contribution to the
Government's reform program and to coordinate with conditions emphasized by other CGAB donors.
Conditionality is focused only on critical actions which would move the Government's reform agenda forward.
Eight triggers have been identified for PRSG-10 inpartnership with the Government. The prior actions have also
beencoordinatedwith the program the Governmentdiscusses with the IMF.

Principle 5; Conduct transparentprogress reviews conducivetopredictable and performance-based financial
support
The CGAB monitors and evaluates permanently progress made with regard to policy implementation and
conducts the above described three annual review sessions. These three sessions are chaired by the Minister of
Economy and Finance, supported by a secretariat made up of a representative of the administration and two
representativesof the donors. The three sessions are open to interested parties, andthe principalresults as well as
a summary of the discussions are documented and publicly available. The Government incorporates feedback
from suchCGAB meetingsinto its own reporting, including for the Annual PRSPProgress Report.




                                                           - 44 -

                             VI. PRSG-9 IMPLEMENTATION

A.       PARTICIPATION PROCESS

 111.    The design of PRSG-9benefitedfrom broad stakeholders consultations, includingin
the context of the new CAS preparation. The Government led several meetings in which
experts from central and line Ministries and top officials, including Ministers, consulted local
Governments officials, private sector representatives, and civil society organizations. Among
those meetings, one can note:


0   the three CGAB-CSLP annual reviews, which represented the last stages of a consultation
    process involving Government experts, private sector and civil society representatives,
    working together in the six established sector and thematic commissions (Commissions
    Sectorielles et The'matiques - CST), allowed candid discussions o f the challenges that
    Burkina Faso faces, including on the organization o f the cotton sector, the implementation o f
    decentralizationand the role o f independent watchdog institutions for good governance;

0   intensive engagement through ESW and AAA (whether IDA- or partner-led) to improve
    appropriation o f analytic findings by all actors involved. Several workshops took place,
    including on the results o f the CPIA exercise, on early findings from the Country Economic
    Memorandum on growth and competitiveness, on the design o f the ongoing PER, in the
    conduct o f the joint DSA with the IMF, inthe conduct o fthe Debt Management Performance
    Assessment Tool (DeMPA) and on broad-based DPL consultations.


B.       POVERTY AND SOCIAL IMPACTS

112.     The proposed PRSG-9 is expected to have a significant positive direct impact on
poverty reduction. First, sustaining the competitiveness o f the cotton sector, facilitating e-
commerce and promoting services to SMEs in two peripheral regions o f the country will help
sustain growth and employment creation, particularly in rural areas where most o f the poor live.
Second, improved access to basic services (primary schools, water and sanitation, basic social
and economic infrastructure in secondary urban areas), which is the main focus o f the
decentralizationprogram will have a direct impact on the poor, support their greater participation
in the growth process and sustain progress towards the MDGs. However, as discussed above
there i s a risk that if not accompanied by the provision o f sufficient resources and strengthened
capacity for local Governments to carry out their new responsibilities, the devolution o f basic
service delivery may result in reduced quality and access to these services. To mitigate this,
several donors support decentralization efforts in Burkina Faso, including the ongoing IDA-
financed Administration Capacity Building project, the Community Driven Development
project, and the Urban Capacity Building project. In addition, the recently completed IDA
analytical work on decentralization i s helping the Government fine tune the decentralization
program, particularly with regard to service delivery for the poor. Finally, the strengthening o f
public financial management and o f the fight against corruption i s expected to enhance
efficiency, transparency and accountability in public resource use.         Such measures would
increase value for public money and the country's absorption capacity for external resources.




                                               - 45 -

113.    Some of the cost restructuring measures implemented by SOFITEX could affect a
limited number of households. By consolidating SOFITEX's viability at time o f financial
distress, the reduction in the use o f contractors and the closing o f one ginning unit in Bobo
Dioulasso (see table 4 above) will help preserve most of the 700,000 jobs that are depending on
the production and exports o f fiber cotton. The strict adherence to the price formula adopted in
2007 by the cotton interprofession is a component o f the smoothing fund, which can mitigate
some o f the impact o f declining cotton prices2' provided that the trend allows a recovery in
international prices over the medium-term, Rising international prices result in higher floor
prices for cotton producers at planting season, supplemented post-harvest by a ristourne when
prices continue to rise during the agricultural campaign. In addition, cotton income represents
less than 25 percent o f the total income o f cotton producers, thus most cotton growers have the
possibility to compensate for low cotton prices by diverting some o f their resources to other farm
and nonfarm activities. In addition to PRSG-9 in support o f cotton sector reforms, IDA i s also
assisting rural households with several projects, particularly to reduce their risks and increase
their revenue. In particular, IDA is supporting implementation o f an agricultural project
(PAFASP) to promote diversification beyond cotton. In parallel, given the current challenging
global context IDA is providing technical assistance to the Government to develop social
protection nets for the population.

114.    With respect to the cotton trigger proposed for PRSG-10, an analysis of the poverty
impact of changes in input costs was conducted for the average cotton farms with
nationally representative household survey data (Annex 4). The analysis shows that a 20
percent reduction inthe price o f input at the farm level would result ina 3.2 percent reduction o f
poverty among cotton farmer, equivalent to a reduction of 0.5 percent in overall poverty in the
country. In general, the household level benefits from the fertilizers subsidy will increase with
the size of cotton acreage, as is typical for area-based or volume-based subsidies, and thus its
poverty targeting efficiency may not be optimal. However, as cotton farmers continue to play a
significant food security role inthe economy, positive externalities are expected to benefit rural
net food buyers and urban dwellers.            The cancellation o f the producers' debt overhang i s
expected to benefit particularly asset-poor farmers with no access to animal traction. Earlier
project preparation studies in the sector have indeed shown that in general this category o f
farmers has been more indebtedthan the average (see Table A5.5 inAnnex 5).


C.      ENVIRONMENTAL          ASPECTS

115.    The activities supported by the proposed operation are not likely to have significant
negative effects on the environment, forests, and other natural resources.

116.    In the cotton sector, PRSG-9 supports Government's efforts to improve governance
rules in the sector and transparency o f SOFITEX's operations, while addressing the cotton
sector's crisis most immediate needs. Beyond PRSG-9, the Government's broader strategy in
the context o f external market factors i s anticipated to have a number o f environmental effects


29The floor price for the coming campaignhas beenestablishedas CFAF160kgofseed cotton. A previous analysis
had estimatedthat a20 CFAFkg (13 percent) reduction inthe producer price from CFAF160per kg of seed cotton
to CFAFI4O per kgwould leadto an increase inpoverty of about 3.5 percentagepoints amongcotton producers.
See Annex 6 inIDA report No. 38008-BF (Program document for PRSC-7).


                                                   - 46 -

which are being tracked. In particular, the Government has authorized the scaling up o f GM
cotton seeds to national scale by the 201041 cropping campaign. First, the IDA-financed West
Africa Regional Biosafety Project i s enhancing regulatory capacity to manage environmental
aspects o f this activity. Second, expansion o f this seed technology could result in a reduction in
pesticide use on cotton. Third, land area devoted to cotton production i s anticipated to respond
to net income prospects for the crop; these may improve marginally inthe short-term but are not
expected to return to levels earlier in the decade, and thus although cotton area may expand
beyond levels o f the last two years, this will be on already cultivated arable land, and not to the
levels o f prior years o f peak cotton production. Finally, fertilizer use in aggregate and per
hectare on cotton i s not anticipated to increase as a result o f PRSG-9 supported actions. World
prices have increased dramatically and are anticipated to remain well above levels o f recent
years, and while domestic nominal price increases are for now beingmoderated by subsidies, the
likely medium-termdirection o f fertilizer/cotton price ratio is upward, hence a dampening effect
on fertilizer use for cotton is expected.

117.    Beyondcotton,the support to SMEsthrough the MEB, while not expected to have direct
consequences on the environment, should help providejobs beyond the agricultural sector, which
will contribute to limit pressure on land use. The decentralization measures are coordinated
around the government's strategy (CSMOD) and corresponding three-year action plan, whose
third pillar focuses on improved land, environmental and natural resource management.
Technical assistance from donors will be needed to achieve these objectives but the CSMOD
offers a framework to coordinate donor interventions in this area. The operationalization of the
FPDCT had a positive impact on the environment by financing drain works in secondary urban
centers.

118.    It is important to underscore that Burkina Faso is aware of the importance of
ensuring environmental sustainability and that the Government has tasked the Ministry of
Environment to supervise compliance o f public policies and programs with environmental
regulations. The Government's policy i s addressing climate change, biodiversity, desertification
and sustainable development more generally.           For rural development, the government is
finalizing a policy framework that will improve consistency between productive investments in
the sector and the preservation o f the environment. This measure i s included in the CGAB
matrix for 2009 and will be monitored by the group o f donors.


D.      FIDUCIARY    ASPECTS

119.    The 2001 CFAA and CPAR, and the 2004 HIPC AAP, were key inputs inthe preparation
o fthe Government's budget management reform program (Programme de Re'forme de la Gestion
Budge'taire, PRGB). The Government has also used the findings o f the 2005 CPAR and the
2007 PEFA assessment to update the PRGB and transform it into a PFM reform strategy
(Strate'giede Renforcement des Finances Publiques or SRFP). The SRFP i s supported by most
donors providing budgetary support, and i s a core element o f the M O U for budget support.
Under PRSC-1 to PRSC-8, most recommendations o f the CFAA and CPAR have been
successfully implemented,        including the implementation o f annual audit procedures
recommended by the CFAA. PRSG-9 builds on the achievements under the previous PRSCs by
supporting the further strengthening o f internal and external oversight, and by including
additional measures to enhance policy-based budgeting and budget transparency. In the context


                                                - 47 -

o f PRSG-9 preparation, the Bank has reviewed fiduciary arrangements. Overall, the Bank has
judged implementation performance of the PFM reform program to date and
Government'scommitmentto PFMimprovementsas satisfactory.

120.   The CentralBankof the West African States (BCEAO) is the commoncentralbank
of the West African countries,includingBurkinaFaso. In2005, IMF staff carried out an on-
site safeguards assessment o f the BCEAO and found that progress had been made in
strengthening the BCEAO's safeguard framework since 2002 when the last safeguard assessment
was undertaken. The BCEAO now publishes a full set of audited financial statements, and
improvements have been made to move financial reporting closer to International Financial
Reporting Standards (IFRS). IMF staff noted that the BCEAO has improved the explanatory
notes to the financial statements and further changes are scheduled for the next fiscal year, with a
view toward a gradual alignment with International Accounting Standard (IAS), as adopted
internationally by other central banks. Furthermore, an internal audit charter has been put in
place, mechanisms have been established to improve risk management and risk prevention, and
follow-up on internal and external audit recommendations has been strengthened. The
assessment identified a number o f areas where further steps would help solidify the progress
made. The main recommendations relate to: (i)      improving the external audit process by adopting
a formal rotation policy and further enhancing the transparency o f the financial statements by
adopting IFRS in full; and (ii)enhancing the effectiveness o f the internal audit function by
further strengthening the reporting to management o f the BCEAO. The status report o f the
implementation o f recommendations, received by the World Bank in March 2007, indicated that
some progress was achieved.

121.   Bank financial management specialists reviewed the flow of funds arrangement
between the Central Bank (BCEAO) and the Treasury (DGTCP), taking into account the
findings o f an audit o f the flow o f budgetary funds between the Central Bank and the Treasury
funded by the multi-donor Budget Support Group. The team concluded that current arrangements
are adequate, with fiduciary risks within acceptable risk tolerance levels. However, the team
recommended that the information systems o f these two institutions be linked, so as to minimize
delays and discrepancies in flows reporting, thus eliminating cumbersome reconciliations. The
last audit report on the flows o f funds, which was produced in 2009, included a clean audit
opinion.

E.     DISBURSEMENT       AND AUDITING

122.   The proposed operation would consist of a single tranche of SDR64.6 million
(US$lOO million equivalent, on grant terms) to be available upon effectiveness and
disbursed on the basis of a withdrawal application. Assuming that the withdrawal request is
in Foreign Exchange, the equivalent amount in CFAF reported inthe budgetary system will be
based on the market rate at the date o f the transfer. The Recipient will promptly notify the Bank
by fax or email that such transfer has taken place, and that proceeds have been credited in a
manner satisfactory to the Bank. The closing date o f the operation will be June 30, 2010. The
operation would follow IDA'Sdisbursement procedures for development policy operations and
would not be linked to specific purchases. Once the Financing Agreement becomes effective
and provided that a withdrawal application to that effect has beenreceived, the proceeds will be
deposited by IDA into a Government account at the Central Bank which forms part o f the


                                                - 48 -

country's foreign exchange reserves; as a due diligence measure, IDA will obtain confirmation
from the Government that the amount o f the operation has been accounted for in the country's
budget management system (within 30 days o f disbursement). If, after being deposited in this
account, the proceeds o f the operation are used for excluded expenditures, as defined in the
Financing Agreement, IDA will requirethe Recipient to refund directly to IDA, an amount equal
to the amount o f said payment, promptly upon notice from IDA. Amounts refunded to the Bank
upon such request shall be cancelled. No dedicated account i s required.

F.      SUPERVISION        ARRANGEMENTS

123.    The Ministry of Economy and Finance will be responsible for overall
implementation of the proposed PRSG-9. The Permanent Secretary of the Permanent
Secretariat for the Monitoringo f Financial Programs and Policies (SP-PPF) inthe MEF will lead
the Government's technical team with representatives o fthe Ministries o fEconomy andFinance;
Basic Education and Literacy; Secondary Education, Higher Education and Scientific Research;
Agriculture, Water and Fisheries; Commerce, Enterprise Development, and Artisanship;
Livestock        and   Animal      Resources;  Public   Administration   and    Decentralization;
Telecommunications and New Technologies; External Control Institutions and representatives o f
public enterprises.

124.     IDA'Ssupervision effort will be aligned with the Government-led monitoring and
evaluation system underpinning the PRSP-11, the Priority Action Plan, and the General
Budget Support Framework (CGAB). The specific benchmarks and outcomes to be
monitored under PRSG-9 (see Annex 3) are extracted from the Government overall development
program (Annex 14), thus providing significant benefits in reducing transaction costs for the
Government. They are included inthejoint performance matrix under the CGAB. IDA staff has
been working with the authorities and the other donors under the CGAB to ensure that
coordination o f the supervision efforts will increase predictability and aid alignment with the
budget cycle. In assessing the implementation o f the PRSP-11, the CGAB will take into account
factors outside Government's control that may alter the underlying assumptions for a successful
PRSP implementation. Such factors include further declining terms of trade, weather-related
shocks, and shortfalls inexternal assistance and regional instability.


G.       R I S K S AND R I S K S MITIGATION

125.     Several risks couldjeopardizethe expected outcomes and benefits of this operation.
A further deterioration of the global financial environmentconstitutesthe most prevalent
risk, for the cotton sector in particular but also for the rest of the economy. Volatility in
commodity prices, particularly cotton, oil and gold, lower capital flows from FDI and ODA and
weaker demand for Burkinabk exports will affect revenue generation, growth and public
finances, as well as private sector activity. The proposed operation would partially mitigate some
o f these risks by providing continuing funding to PRSP-I1 implementation. Moreover, the focus
o f the current program on restoring balances inthe cotton sector and improving transparency and
financial management i s designed to help Burkina Faso cushion the effects o f economic
volatility and protect critical expenditure in the current context o f limited resources.      The
Government i s accompanied inthis task by the series o f PRSCs, other IDA and donors supported



                                                - 4 9 -

operations and AAA to improve the financial capacity o f cotton companies, avoid a systemic
crisis from the build-up of producers' debts, improve alternatives to cotton for farmers and help
develop well targeted safety nets for the population most affected by the crisis.

126.    Moreover, resistance to reforms could slow down the implementation of the
decentralization strategy or delay the necessary controls on large public procurement
contracts, affecting aid effectiveness over time. Burkina Faso's highly centralized political
system, top-down hierarchical traditions and the existence o f patron-client relationships in the
state system can create disincentives within political institutions to initiate and sustain reforms.
While this risk is difficult to mitigate, the PRSC series is based on reforms agreed inthe context
o f the government-donors harmonization framework, with an emphasis on budget transparency,
the development o f local governments, and the strengthening of external controls. Inparallel,
the World Bank i s providing technical assistance to improve country systems, particularly for
public procurements.

127.    Public sector capacity constraints can constitute a challenge to successful
implementation of the reform program. In particular, the well-organized and professional
highlevel administration can be contrasted with weak lower level administrative capacity. The
proposed operation seeks to mitigate this risk in several ways: first, the operation has a focused
coverage in terms o f policy areas and number o f reform measures; second, it emphasizes
building on ongoing initiatives as opposed to introducing new ones; and third, it relies
extensively on Bank and other donors instruments for alleviating capacity constraints as
discussed insection IV.

128.    In IDA'Sassessment, the potential benefits of the proposed operation outweigh the
residual risks and warrant IDA'Sassistance for implementing critical reforms and policy actions
ina coordinated fashion with other donors, while supporting risk mitigation actions to maximize
the sustainability o fthe reform agenda.

MINISTRY OF ECONOMY

No2009-            /MEF/CAB/SP-PPF

                                                                 1818, HStreet,
                                                                 WASHINGTON DC, 20433 (USA)
  Subject:Letter of DevelopmentPolicy

  Mr.President,
  1.This letter of development policy (LDP) is an account of Burhna Faso's economic and social situation in
  2008 and its outlook for the next two years. It i s mainly based on the revised Poverty Reduction Strategy
  Paper (PRSP) and its 2008-2010 Priority Action Plan (PAP) whose d e t d s of implementation have been spelt
  out in the 2008 implementation report. The letter focuses on the implementation of Government's priority
  reforms supported by the ninthPoverty Reduction Support Grant (PRSG) of the WorldBank.


         1 IMPLEMENTATION STATUS OF THE POVERTYREDUCTIONSTRATEGY
  PAPER
  2. Despite the difficult domestic economic situation in 2008 due to exogenous shocks, signs of economic
  recovery are beginning to be seen with an estimated real GDP growth rate of 5 percent, up from 3.6 percent
  in 2007. This growth, after the over 40 percent decline in 2007, is the result of the 29 percent increase in
  cotton production, thanks to favorable weather conditions, the dynamism of the mining sector following the
  goinginto operation of three gold mines and the continued implementation of fiscal and structural reforms.
  Grain production i s said to have exceeded 4 d o n tons while that of cotton and gold stood at 500 000 tons
  and 5.375 tons respectively.

  3. Inflationary pressures have, however, remained strong despite the .good crop year. Indeed, the inflation
  rate w as estimated at 10.7 as against -0.3 percent in 2007, representing a net increase from one year to
  another, as a result of expenses related to the three main functions of consumption, namely     ((food and non-
  alcoholic beverages  D, ((transport))and the item   ((housing, water, gas and other fuels)).T h e budget deficit,
  based on commitments exclusive of grants, i s said to have substantially dropped from 12.2 percent to 8.5
  percent.


  1.1        ACCELERATINGECONOMICGROWTHAND BASINGIT ON EQUITY
 4. The State budget remains the ideal framework for financing the implementation of PRSP priorities, which
 include Mdlennium Development Goals. Concerns relating thereto have been taken into account in the 2009
 appropriation law which was designed in keeping with the sector ceilings set by the M e d i u m T e r m
 Expenditure Framework (MTEq for 2009-2011.              T h e 2009 appropriation law, adopted by the National
 Assembly on December 4, 2008, laid down the following priorities: 1) deepening the fight against poverty; 2)
 revival of the production sectors of the economy; 3) promotion of employment; 4) combating fraud and
 corruption; 5) enhancing security and the promotion of human rights, 6) continuing of State reforms and
  finally; 7) implementation of the total cornmunabation of the territory.



                                                        - 53 -

5. Regarding refosms in the cotton sector, the Government in collaboration with players of the sector,
continued its efforts to implement the strategy for ending the crisis and reviving the sector. In 2008, the
Burkina Faso Inter-professional Cotton Association (AICB) was encouraged to strictly apply the mechanism
for setting the cotton prices authorized under the rules governing the smoothing fund, and SOFITEX urged
to implement the plan to reduce its operating costs and carry out the programmed operational and financial
audts. These measures were globally properly carried out and SOFITEX was able to reduce its operating
costs by some 5 billion CFA francs. T h e price of inputs was stabilized at its 2007-2008 level through a budget
subsidy of 6.5 billton CFA francs. Following a call for applications, the Association of Smoothing Funds
(AFdL,) also entrusted the management of the smoothing funds to the Bank of Africa w h c h henceforth will
be responsible for fixing producer prices in keeping with the approved parameters but at the same time
adjusting the prices to those of world prices for cotton. It should b e also noted that a contract was signed
with the Global Credit Rating Co. for the (virtual) financial rating of SOFITEX in order to provide the
managers of the company with the elements for improvingtheir internal governance.

6. In the area of information and communication technologies, Government adopted and tabled before the
National Assembly a draft bill laying down the rules governing electronic transactions and services. This law
willcreate confidence .andthe legalsecurity for electronic exchanges.
7. In order to strengthen the legal and institutional environment for business, Government undertook to
strengthen achevements and take further actions, among which are, notably, the creation and operation of a
one-stop window for issuingland titles, the setting up of the Center for the fachtation of budding documents
(CEFAC) in Ouagadougou and Bobo-Dioulasso, the puttinginto place with the "Maison de YEntreprise du
B u r h n a Faso (MEBF)" of an exports support window, the creation of a business clunate Observatory, the
devolution of CEFORE to the Center East (Tenkodogo) and North (Ouahigouya) regions and the creation
of a national card-index for the Register of Commerce and Personal Property Loans PCCM).


      1.2      ENSURINGACCESS OF THE POORTO BASIC SOCIAL SERVICESAND SOCIAL
                                                 PROTECTION

8. Given the objectives of the second pillar of the PRSP, namely, the development of human resources to
sustain economic growth and the creation of jobs, this section highlights the basic social sectors, notably basic
education and health, social protection, drinking water and sanitation. All the sector reforms under this
framework are geared towards the taking into account of dsadvantaged groups.


                                 1.2.1 ACCESS TOBASICEDUCATION
9. During 2008, Government actions with regard to access to basic education (preschool, primary, non-
formal education) and post-primary education (secondary education, vocational training and higher
education) mainly focused on (i)     the expansion of educational opportunities and vocational training, (ii)
improving the quality, relevance and effectiveness of education and vocational training and (iii) improving
and strengthening the management and monitoring capacities of institutions.

10. In terms of impact on primary education, the implementation of the reform of the education system has
been satisfactory at national level, with a 49 878 increase in enrolment in 2007/2008), roughly twice the
number for 2006/2007 (26 348). This significant increase in school enrolment has positively influenced the
evolution of the gross enrolment rate which rose from 66.5 percent to 71.2 percent in 2007/2008. The
number of new comers into year one of primary school (CP1) at national level which was 346 961 pupils
(45.94 percent for p l s ) in 2006/2007, rose to 388 889 (47.01 percent for girls) in2007/2008, representing an
increase rate of 12.08 percent. Overall enrolment of CP1 pupils thus increased from 11561 258 (including
699 842 girls) in 2006/2007 to 1742 439 (including 794 594 girls) in 2007/2008. From one school year to
another, the percentage of p l s in school enrolment increased from 44.8 percent to 45.6 percent, representing
an increase of 0.8 points.




                                                     - 54 -

11.Government, in pursuit of PDDEB goals, is makmg efforts to implement phase I1of the PDDEB.To
improve educational opportunities, it has maintained its policy of free distributionof textbooks. This has led
to a scaling up of the efficiency ratios of the education system of an arithmetic bookperpupil))and
                                                                          ((                             ((a reading
bookperpttpil)),as these ratios rose between 2007 and 2008 respectively from 0.73 to 0.91 and from 0.58 to
0.90. Efforts to increase access to education have focused on enhancing the development of preschool,
primary and post- primary education.

12.As concerns decentralization, activities in thus area were carried out under the 2008-2010 three-year action
plan of the strategy framework for the implementation of decentralization (CSMOD) adopted inJune 2008
by the National Conference on Decentralization (CONAD).Government began to specify the framework for
the transfer of posers and resources by takmg 5 decrees and s i p g memoranda of operation with councils,
in particular in the areas of basic education, drinkmg water, sanitation and health In conformity with t h s
framework, resources have already been transferred to cover operating expenses, maintenance of school
infrastructure in the area of basic education in 49 urban councils as well as funds for the construction of new
school infrastructure in 13 council regional headquarters. Funding for               the Permanent Fund for the
Development of Local Authorities, (F'PDCT), was ensured in the 2008 amendmg finance act by the allocation
of 5 billion CFA francs for that purpose This provision, which was extended in the 2009 budget, is made
available to the Fundon a quarterly basis.

                                    1.2.2 ACCESS TOHEALTH SERVICES

13. In order to increase health coverage in the health sector, Government, with the support of its technical
and financial partners, during 2008 made efforts to build new health facrlities (dialysis center, the Banfora
CHR (Regional Hospital Center), the 600 bed CHU (University Teaching Hospital) of excellence in
Ouagadougou, the Bobo-Dioulasso pediatric hospital and the Bobo-Dioulasso and Fada N'Gourma centers
for the psychological care of women victims of fistula).

14. In order to improve the quality and use of health services, n e w CSPS were provided with Essential
Generic Drugs (EGD). An analysis of the situation in hospital pharmacies was also carried out and
performance contracts incorporating various aspects (ensuring quality care, hospital hygiene, hospital
information system, monitoring deliveries in delivery room) signed between the M i n i s t r y of Health and the
CHU and CHR reference hospitals.

                          1.2.3 ACCESS TODRINKING WATERAND SANITATION

15. In the area of drinkmg water, government policy i s focused on increasing access to water and sanitation
facilities in both the urban and rural areas. The policy on drinkingwater supply in rural areas i s based on the
sharing of roles between the State and the beneficiaries. The State i s responsible for the building of water
facdities and their rehabilitation while the recipient population i s responsible for the maintenance of the
facilities. T h i s division of roles i s to ensure the sustainabdity of the fachties. Aware of the importance of
access to hnking water for the health and well-being of the population, Government decided to include
dnnkmg water among the areas transferred to local authorities as part of its decentralization policy. In this
context, memorandums of operation were signed with 100 rural councils for the management of water
facilities.

16.As regards sanitation, Government continued the implementation of its wastewater and excreta treatment
strategy. In rural areas, the rates of sanitation facilities built in schools are 28.21 percent (with almost a third
reserved for girls) 24.28 percent for health centers, 11.6 percent for public latrines and 89.44 percent for
family latrines. T h e equipment rates are around 58.22 percent for schools, 75.79 percent for CSPS and 41.97
percent for public places. T h e access rate to f a d y sanitation i s 17.03percent.

                                       ACTIVITIES FORTHE POOR

17. This third component of the PRSP and its priority reforms program focus on the capacity buddingof the
poor to generate income by promotingtheir participation in the productionprocess.

18. Inlight of the high cost of living, reflected in 2008 by the surge inthe prices of essential goods, especially
agricultural products, Government developed and implemented, with the support of its technical and
 financial partners, an emergency plan for the achievement of food and nutrition security. T h e actions under
 this plan gave impetus to the efforts already being made to promote the rural world. They have produced
very significant results in terms of increased food production.

19. Government also supported the development of income-generating activities for the most vulnerable
 social groups, through the continued implementation; in particular, of the action plans of the national micro

1.4    PROMOTING GOOD GOVERNANCE

20. Sipficant progress has been made in the area of governance. The improvement of public finance
management mechanisms and the decentrahation process remained, in this regard, at the forefront of
Government priorities throughout 2008. The main activities in this axis focused on the consolidation of
achievements.

21. As regards public finances, activities inthis area focused on the implementation of the three-year sectoral
action plans (PAST) of the strategy to enhance public finances (SRFP), under the supervision of the
monitoring fachty set up in early 2008. The organs of this facility functioned in accordance with the
provisions governing them, includmg the holding of periodic meetings of the Steering Committee and the
work of technical groups. 2008's balance sheet reviewed as of February 19,2009, shows an overall satisfactory
implementation of the PAST-SRFP annual tranche for 2008, particularly in the areas of (i)        budget forecast
and programming, (ii) resource mobilization, (iii)   optimization of budget execution procedures, (iv) control,
(v) devolution and decentralization and (vi) capacity building.

22. Significant progress has been made in the implementation of the reforms planned in the area of public
finances in2008.

23. The fiscal policyreform process started insince 2007 attained a major d e s t o n e with the adoption by the
Council of Ministers on October 1, 2008 of the strategy paper developed under the auspices of the fiscal
policy committee set up to oversee the formulation of reforms and their implementation. The new short term
tax measures, which mainly concern the elunination of deductions at source and levies on large companies, as
well as broadening the scope of excise duties have been included inthe 2009 appropriation law.

24. The new public procurement rules adopted inDecember 2007 came into force onJuly 1,2008. The focus
i s now on (i)  building the legal framework in conformity with international standards and those of the
community (UEMOA) through the adaptation and Qssemination of regulations and standard documents.,
and (ii)  the strengthening of the institutional framework and public procurement capacities through the
adoption and implementation of a national strategy for strengthening the sector's capacities and rendering the
SIMT operational on a pilot basis in three ministries and three regions. The effective going into operation of
the A    W and the devolution of the DGMT should further accelerate the progress made in the area of
public procurement.

25. As regards the control and combating of corruption, progress made in 2008 include : (i) effective

                              2.      PROSPECTSFORTHE 2009- 2010 PERIOD

27. Government will continue its efforts to render operational the poverty reduction strategy paper, whose

                   2.1    ACCELERATING ECONOMIC GROWTH BASED O N EQUITY

28. At the macroeconomic level, the second PRGF program review conducted in March/April 2008
reiterated the need for the implementation of the measures stated in the three core priorities of the SRFP
(control system, public procurement and domestic taxation) to strengthen public finances and the basis of
economic growth. Regarding the latter, emphasis will be placed on the promotion of the private sector and
improving the competitiveness of the economy through the design, as part of the sector policy of the
M i n i s t r y of Commerce, of a national quality policy for putting in place a national system for the
standardization, certification and accreditation of Burkina Faso products with a view to improving their
access to the international market.

29. Government will pursue the implementation of the strategy to end the crisis and revive the cotton sector,
notably through the development of a 2009-2011 three-year action plan by September 2009 to restructure and
support the sector. Government wdl be responsible for monitoring the implementation of this plan through
quarterly performance reports. It wdl start the effective establishment of a consultation framework.           The
reports wdl contain the measures taken to improve productivity (including an inputs supply policy), the work
out and the efforts made to strengthen SOFITEX'sperformance.

30. Duringits Council of Ministers' meeting of May 6,2009, Government decided to extend the state subsidy
for the purchase of inputs and settle the outstanding debts of cotton producer groups. 'Confident of this
support, the Inter-professiorial CottonAssociation of Burkma Faso announced a cotton seed purchase price
of 160 CFA Frs./kgand the sale price of agricultural inputs for the 2009-2010 crop year.

31. Inorder to reduce the dependence of the economy on the cotton sector, Government launched a series of
initiatives to diversify exports. In this perspective, the national export strategy currently being finalized wdl set
the major guidelines in h s area. However, pending these broad guidehes, Government has taken concrete
measures in some sectors such as livestock-meat where it i s doing all, on the one hand, to scale up the
utilization of the Ouagadougou refrigerated slaughterhouse (AFO) and increase meat export as opposed to
cattle on the hoof and, on the other, enhance i t s attractiveness to the private sector. I t wdl thus draw up the
terms of reference for the recruitment of a technical partner of choice for the management of the
Ouagadougou refrigerated slaughterhouse and launch the recruitment notice Meeting the required standards,
an essential factor for the competitiveness of the AFO, will be supported by the World Bank through the
support project for the agricultural and forestry sectors OAFASP).



                                                        - 57 -

32. As regards the improvement of the business climate, Government plans to carry out several activities
during the year 2009. Indeed, it will develop an integrated system for the management of CEFORE's
activities, and will pursue the devolution of CEFORE in the Cenue-North, East, Centre-West and South-
West regions so as to improve the services offered to businesses, and, more generally, the business
environment in a bid to better simplify procedures and reduce administrative costs for the exercise of
business.. To do this, it will support the implementation of the priority measures of the strategic plan of the
Maison de YEntreprise duBurkina Faso (MEBF) with a view to ensuring the sustainability of the structure.

33. Inthe area of food security, the government intends to continue i t s efforts to ensure the enjoyment by the
population of this fundamental right Apart from the annual budgetary allocations for the replenishment of
the security stock and the proper functioning of the information system on food security and nutrition, it will
continue its support efforts to increase grain production, particularly under the framework of the initiative for
achieving food security.   . Specialemphasis will be laid, in this respect, on the access of inputs and improved
seeds as well as on the development of rice lowlands and the intensification of out-of-season cultivation.

   2.2       ENSURINGACCESS BYTHE POOR TO BASICSOCIAL SERVICESAND SOCIAL

action plan, strengthened by the F T I Program, to achieve the goals of the   ((Education For All )) Initiative and
the Millennium Development Goals and the objectives of the education policy whose target is to achieve
universal primary education and universal completion by the end of the next decade.. Government will also
continue to apply the policy of free basic education ingovernment schools, in keeping with the guidance law.

36. In the health sector, Government, that has already started the implementation of the contracts strategy
between the Ministry of Health and all the structures involved in the sector, will start putting in place its
monitoring-evaluation facility.

37. The process to decentraltze social sectors dsee significant progress with i)the inclusion in the 2010
appropriation law of financial resources to be transferred, on the one hand, to the P D C T and, on the other,
to local authorities for recurrent costs, includmg school funds, in basic education and for rehabilitation
investments in the AEPA area, concerning infrastructure expenses related to the powers transferred.. With
regard to the FTDCT, this will involve the transfer to the structure once again in 2010 of at least 5 billion to
support the financial needs of local authorities for priority investments. At the level of basic education, the

includmg the drinkingwater and wastewater and excreta treatment sectoral dunension of the PCD-AEPA.

urban councils.

                            2.4      PROMOTING GOOD GOVERNANCE

42. As regards local governance, the Government remains committed to the effective implementation of
decentralization, deemed essential for promoting participation of the population at the grassroots in the
development of Burkma Faso. It intends thus to strengthen the process, notably by accelerating the transfer
of powers and resources to local authorities. E-governance and the technical framework for the information
and communication technology will be strengthened, in particular through information and communication
technology, notably through the development of 2 (two) sectoral e-strategies to promote the integration of
ICTin sectoral development policies.

43. With regard to reforms inthe public finance sector, efforts dfocus on the implementation of the 2009
tranche of the three-year sectoral action plan of the strategy to strengthen public fmances (SRFP), by
capitahing, in particular, on the achievements of the consultations within the General Framework for the
organization of Budgetary Supports in support of the PRSP (CGAB-PRSP)in order to maintain substantial
budgetary aid flows sizeable enough to support the implementation of the priority actions program of the
PRSP. There are plans to merge the CGAB-PRSP matrix with the PAP-PRSP to fachtate monitoring at the
level of all those involved in the process.

44. In the area of control and the fight against corruption, Government will strengthen the control services
carried out by the Directorate General of Financial Control by utilizing the decentrahed services of the
structure. It will support the setting up of its budgetary and financial dscipline Chamber of the State Audit
Office which plans to start investigating cases by the end of the year.

45. As regards the strengthening of transparency inthe management system of public finances, some essential
budgetary and accounting information will be made available on the website of the M i n i s t r y of Economy and
Finance. This action dthus expand public access to budgetary information, following the publication of the
budget in the mainnational languages.

46. The budget formulation procedure wdl also continue to be refined in order to ensure a closer link with
the PRSP. In h s respect, Government i s implementing a reform program for budget programming whose
ultimate goal i s the full implantation of the program budget by 2012. During the 2008-2011 transitional
period, Government will work towards strengthening ownership of programming tools and will experiment
programming laws with some ministries of the priority sectors. As regards public procurement, priority shall
be given to the devolution of the DGMP and to the deployment of S I M P in order to improve the
procurement system.

47. Work on the Comprehensive Survey of the Living Conditions of Households (EICVM) dcontinue up
to 2010 to establish a new povertyprofile and adapt the sustainable development policy of the country.

48. The government remains convinced that with WorldBank support, through the tenth Poverty Reduction
Support Grant (PRSG-lo), the satisfactory implementation of the priority actions will contribute to the
consolidation of the macroeconomic framework and the sustainment of a stronger, healthy and long lasting
economic growth necessary for a significant reduction of poverty.
